href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":608656,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":1080,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/2r/rd/p02rrdds.jpg","SourceWidth":1920,"SynopsisLong":"(Credit: Getty Images)","SynopsisMedium":"(Credit: Getty Images)","SynopsisShort":"(Credit: Getty Images)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/2r/rd/p02rrdds.jpg","Title":"sections_03_technology_gettyimages_1920.jpg","CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p02rrdds","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p02rrdds","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p02rrdds","_id":"559a699c82f2d2dbf7bd358d"}],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Chris Wright is a problem solver. Her clients come to her with an issue, a question, a mystery, and she figures out the best way to find the answer &ndash; using whatever tools she can. &ldquo;I use a combination of new technology and old technology, because I have to solve a problem. So I&rsquo;ve used everything from geese and dogs to Roombas to drones to GPS.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Wright is a private investigator &ndash; and owner of the Wright Group &ndash; based in Anaheim, California. She&rsquo;s worked in the business for more than 40 years, and has seen the tools available to investigators change dramatically. Early on, stakeouts in vans were important. More recently new technology in the form of tiny cameras and social media has begun to play a role. And she&rsquo;s embraced those changes. Today, when the problem calls for it, she uses drones to do her work.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;She gives me a few examples. If two people are meeting in a public place, a drone can be a helpful way to discreetly watch them. &ldquo;We stay at about 50-75 feet [15-23 metres] above so nothing can be heard.&rdquo; Drones are also helpful for aerial surveillance of locations that are hard to access on foot. And if a school or church is worried someone might be stealing or vandalising property, drones or small off-road vehicles (&ldquo;Roombas on steroids&rdquo; as she calls them) can film the property.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02m7yhk\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In one case, Wright was asked to figure out whether or not a soda salesperson was crossing county lines and cheating on his contract. California is one of many states in which salespeople have regional contracts &ndash; for instance, Bob sells Pepsi in Los Angeles County and Nancy sells Pepsi in Orange County. If Nancy arrives at her usual businesses to sell her Pepsi and finds the soda supply has already been topped up, there&rsquo;s a good chance that someone (perhaps Bob) has crossed county lines and sold illegally.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Hi-tech toys&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Wright was asked to figure out whether this was happening. To do so meant visiting every major soda wholesaler from San Luis Obispo to San Diego &ndash; about 300 miles (480km) of California coast &ndash; and checking whether any were selling soda from the wrong salesperson. When there was illegal soda on sale, she would use a drone to follow the soda delivery trucks back to their depots. In one case, the warehouse the truck led her back to was out in the desert and would have been impossible to approach by car or foot without being noticed. But the drone was able to spy on the trucks covertly. &ldquo;We could see between the warehouse door and the truck loading.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Wright gets her drones from high-end toy stores, for about $200 each. They&rsquo;re an expensive investment: not only do you have to buy the device, you also have to pay one or two people to pilot and spot the thing. And if you lose one during a mission, you&rsquo;re out a good chunk of your budget. But it can be worth it, because for the cases in which they&rsquo;re useful, they can be very useful indeed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02m7yh4\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Wright doesn&rsquo;t pilot the drones herself. &ldquo;I try to hire gamers. I go to the colleges and high schools and I find out who the geeks are, and then I hire them.&rdquo; She said that her pilots are more skilled than she would ever be &ndash; and they like the challenge. Some of them are working towards their own private investigator licences, and their hours piloting the little devices can count as hours towards their certification. (None of Wright&rsquo;s gamer pilots were willing to talk for this article. &ldquo;They&rsquo;re introverts,&rdquo; she told me. &ldquo;Not shy, but introverts.&rdquo;)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Understandably, the idea of using drones to spy on people isn&rsquo;t something everybody is comfortable with. &lt;a href=\"http://www.capitolhillseattle.com/2013/05/chs-x-files-capitol-hill-drone-pilot-spotted-glowing-orbs-phone-thief-on-wheels/\"&gt;In a case in Seattle&lt;/a&gt; in 2013, a woman reported that someone was using a drone to spy on her. &ldquo;This afternoon, a stranger set an aerial drone into flight over my yard and beside my house near Miller Playfield,&rdquo; she told the Capitol Hill Seattle Blog. &ldquo;I initially mistook its noisy buzzing for a weed-whacker on this warm spring day. After several minutes, I looked out my third-story window to see a drone hovering a few feet away.&rdquo; Her husband asked the drone operator, who was standing nearby, to move along &ndash; but the operator claimed to be acting within his legal rights.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tightening regulations&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Whether that&rsquo;s true isn&rsquo;t always clear. &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncsl.org/research/civil-and-criminal-justice/current-uas-state-law-landscape.aspx\"&gt;According to the National Conference of State Legislatures&lt;/a&gt;, 35 states considered adding drone bills to the books last year, and 10 states actually did add new laws. In Iowa, for example, it&rsquo;s now illegal for the state to use drones to enforce traffic laws. In North Carolina, no one can use a drone for surveillance of a person or private property. And Tennessee now specifies that it&rsquo;s a misdemeanour to use drones for surveillance of people who are hunting or fishing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Wright&rsquo;s drone operations might soon become legally questionable too. Earlier this month, a California senator &lt;a href=\"http://www.kcra.com/news/calif-lawmakers-consider-drone-privacy-bill/31326004\"&gt;introduced a bill&lt;/a&gt; that would extend property rights into airspace, meaning that drones flying over private property would be considered trespassers. Just a few days before that, President Obama and the Federal Aviation Administration &lt;a href=\"http://www.forbes.com/sites/gregorymcneal/2015/02/15/a-brief-summary-of-the-faas-new-drone-regulations/\"&gt;announced new drone regulations&lt;/a&gt; as well, requiring &ndash; among other things &ndash; that drones must be under 55lb (25kg) and that operators must keep the flying vehicles in sight at all times.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02m7xlr\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Because the laws are murky, many private investigators steer clear of drones. &ldquo;The use of drones for surveillance is highly restricted by law,&rdquo; said Kelly Riddle, a private investigator in Texas. &ldquo;There are air space regulations as well as privacy laws that can easily be violated. Obtaining video using a drone has thus far been something that we have been advised is illegal.&rdquo; That&rsquo;s because drones are often used to observe activities that can&rsquo;t be seen via a direct line of sight at ground level. Going out of your way to spy on such activities is considered an invasion of privacy, says Riddle. A lot of Wright&rsquo;s work sidesteps this privacy question, because it involves helping schools and churches monitor their own property.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In all likelihood, the use of drones will be restricted under a more comprehensive set of rules and regulations in the United States sooner than later. But in the meantime Wright will continue to use them when they can help with her work. But she also says that regardless of the legality, if someone thinks their privacy is being compromised, they&rsquo;re going to do something about it. That can mean shooting down drones &ndash; another activity that &lt;a href=\"http://www.popsci.com/article/technology/man-shoots-down-drone-lawyers-scratch-their-heads\"&gt;may or may not be legal&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;I think a lot of my colleagues have lost them and realised that it is a tool, and if you invade someone&rsquo;s privacy, well, if they can hit it they will.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Share this story on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;, &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/bbc_future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2015-03-18T11:27:45Z","HeadlineLong":"The private investigator who spies using drones","HeadlineShort":"I use a drone to spy on people","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Aerial vehicles are the latest tool for private detectives  but for how long? Rose Eveleth reports.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Aerial vehicles are the latest tool for private detectives  but for how long? Rose Eveleth reports.","SummaryShort":"Confessions of a modern private eye","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-03-18T11:27:45Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"15e5f7ec-d1ea-438c-b51f-af739ffa5ad1","Id":"wwfuture/story/20150318-i-use-a-drone-to-spy-on-people","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-03-18T11:27:45Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20150318-i-use-a-drone-to-spy-on-people"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20150318-i-use-a-drone-to-spy-on-people","_id":"559a6a6482f2d2dbf7bd51c2"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s good to keep in touch &ndash; something that Edi Haug and Laura Schwengber know only too well. The pair have been friends since childhood. Within the first year of their friendship, nerve damage inflicted by an inherited genetic condition robbed Haug of his sight and hearing. He was nine. But children are natural innovators. &ldquo;We started to invent languages and forms of communication just because we were kids and we wanted to play,&rdquo; says Schwengber. &ldquo;It was annoying that he couldn't hear me, and I couldn't write something down,&rdquo; she says. So the two invented their own tactile language.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As Haug and Schwengber grew older, they replaced their private language with one that is more widely understood: Lorm, a tactile alphabet spelled out with strokes to the hand. It was invented in the nineteenth century by Hieronymus Lorm &ndash; an anonymous pseudonym, for Austrian-born poet, journalist, and novelist &lt;a href=\"http://www.jewishvirtuallibrary.org/jsource/judaica/ejud_0002_0013_0_12762.html\"&gt;Heinrich Landesmann&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; and is still used today by the deaf-blind community in German-speaking countries.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02lv0k9\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s difficult to overstate the importance of Lorm to Haug and Schwengber. But Lorm, and other forms of tactile signing, have their limits. A reliance on physical contact between communicators has historically constrained the social circles of the deaf-blind to those they can meet face-to-face (or more accurately hand-to-hand) on a regular basis &ndash; and even within this relatively small group of people, only those who take the time to learn and practice Lorm can really become part of a deaf-blind individual&rsquo;s social network.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For instance, Haug&rsquo;s social circle currently consists of about five people: his mother, Schwengber, teachers, and therapists. Once a year he spends about 10 days visiting relatives in Stuttgart in Southern Germany. &ldquo;The first five days they need to practice Lorming, so they can remember the letters, and by the tenth day when they are quite fast, it's time to go back home,&rdquo; explains Schwengber.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But these drawbacks might soon disappear, if a new invention by Tom Bieling is commercialised.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tactile translation&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Bieling, a researcher at the Design Lab in Berlin, has developed a &lt;a href=\"http://www.design-research-lab.org/projects/mobile-lorm-glove/\"&gt;glove kitted out with fabric pressure-sensors&lt;/a&gt;. By translating a tactile hand touch alphabet into digital text, the mobile glove could eliminate the necessity of hand-to-hand physical contact for deaf-blind communication. Even better, because a great deal of online communication is text-based, the glove could act as a translating device that allows people who are deaf-blind to communicate freely with anyone &ndash; and for anyone to communicate with them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It's an invention reminiscent of the outlandish gadgets seen in James Bond movies, and it won Bieling first prize in the 2014 Falling Walls Lab competition &ndash; a kind of TED Talks meets Dragon&rsquo;s Den &ndash; held annually in Berlin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02lv1kq\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The first prototype was made from Gore-Tex fabric and felt much like an ordinary glove, Bieling explains. It&rsquo;s embedded with small vibrating motors, and &ldquo;as soon as you receive an incoming message, it starts vibrating on those dots where the letters are positioned&rdquo;, he says. In the current version, users can adjust the intensity and speed of the incoming tactile messages according to their reading skills.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What&rsquo;s more, the whole hand area of the glove is now wired for sensory input, &ldquo;just like a tablet [computer]&rdquo;, he says. &ldquo;The system recognises both the position and pattern of the finger movement.&rdquo; So users can also spell out their own messages.If a sign isn&rsquo;t &ldquo;typed&rdquo; quite correctly, the Lorm glove system recognises the closest symbol &ndash; just like a spell checker on your smart phone. For example, a circle in the hand would be an &lsquo;S&rsquo;, explains Bieling, and even if you type a triangle or a square, the system is clever enough to recognise the letter &lsquo;S&rsquo; as the closest correct symbol.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Enhancing independence&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Speaking through Schwengber&rsquo;s translation, Haug describes the glove prototype as a bit slower than what he is used to in terms of receiving messages. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s like his teacher or his mother Lorming,&rdquo; says Schwengber, compared with her own speedy pace. &ldquo;But he really likes it,&rdquo; she says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Using the glove, &ldquo;I can send and receive &ndash; it&rsquo;s easy,&rdquo; says Haug.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Bieling&rsquo;s project is part of his doctoral studies exploring links between design and ability. He argues that feeling disabled is a question of design &ndash; a ramp, for instance, is easy for anyone to ascend, even if they are in a wheelchair. He hopes that his design will enable deaf-blind people to engage with a broader spectrum of their community and &ldquo;gain access to a broader range of information, thus enhancing their independence&rdquo;. But he also hopes that gaining knowledge about alternative communication systems can profit everyone.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02lv1wv\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Haug, now 22, currently lives with his mother in Spreewald, about 100km south of Berlin. He is studying massage therapy in the hopes of pursuing it as a profession. He would like to move to Berlin, explains Schwengber, but it&rsquo;s a possibility that has thus far eluded him because of the lack of assistance available. The glove could expand Haug&rsquo;s ability to stay in close touch with those closest to him while living at a distance. But it could do more than that: it could allow him to expand his currently small social circle dramatically.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;World in your hands&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Lately, Haug, with help from Schwengber, has been having fun on Twitter, a medium enabling him to converse with people who need never know that he is deaf-blind. On social media, Haug is &ldquo;just a person behind the Twitter account saying something&rdquo;, explains Schwengber. Haug describes it as a bit like playing carnival, because &ldquo;you just mask up and say anything you want&rdquo;, translates Schwengber. &ldquo;Content matters, not the person,&rdquo; she explains. So Haug gets a kick out of the reaction from followers when he Tweets amusing things, something he&rsquo;d be able to do more easily and independently with his own Lorm glove.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Asked about the first thing he would say with a Lorm glove of his own, Haug says he would talk to his cousin in Stuttgart and ask him if he can remember the name of an interesting film they recently spoke about. (Schwengber and Haug often go to the cinema &ndash; where Schwengber simultaneously translates the movie for Haug via Lorm).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Most of us take for granted the digital revolution and the amazing new connections it has offered. For those constrained by a barrier of unseen sights and unheard sounds, it was once unexplored territory &ndash; but with the Lorm glove, they might just have that world in the palm of their hands.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Share this story on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;, &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/bbc_future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2015-03-13T09:29:01Z","HeadlineLong":"The glove that transmits touch over the internet","HeadlineShort":"The glove turning touch into talk","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Blind-deaf people are often forced to live in a small and isolated world. galleries:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121219-2012-the-year-in-space\"&gt;A year in space&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121219-2012-the-year-in-wildlife\"&gt;The year in wildlife&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121219-2012-weird-and-wonderful\"&gt;Weird and wonderful&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121221-2012-a-year-of-new-frontiers\"&gt;Places&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121221-2012-a-year-of-new-frontiers\"&gt;New frontiers&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this slideshow or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a66a082f2d2dbf7bcb96f"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","HeadlineLong":"CES: Hits and misses of the last 45 years","HeadlineShort":"CES: Hits and misses","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"One of the world's largest technology shows kicks off this week in Las Vegas. The Consumer Electronics Show (CES) brings together 150,000 people and 20,000 new products. \n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s a golden rule in consumer technology: you can&rsquo;t predict the future. Just ask the Popular Mechanics writer who said, in 1949, &ldquo;Computers in the future may weigh no more than 1.5 tons.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But 2013 presents a unique opportunity to make safe predictions. Maybe not about consumer tech, but at least about the annual trade show that celebrates it: the Consumer Electronics Show.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For years, CES was North America&rsquo;s biggest trade show. It&rsquo;s been running in various forms since 1967. Every January, nearly 150,000 attendees walk its 2,700 booths, then wait 45 minutes for taxis so they can return to their overpriced Las Vegas hotel rooms with aching feet.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So what&rsquo;s the prediction? That CES has started a long, slow decline.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;CES is not open to the public. It&rsquo;s attended exclusively by electronics representatives, investors, and members of the media. Its function: for tech companies to display the new gadgets they&rsquo;ve been working on - and to beg the press to write about them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And we do. Every January, we, the press, dutifully report the industry&rsquo;s latest message. This will be the year we surf the web on our TV sets! This will be the year for 3D television! This will be the year someone besides Apple has a hit tablet!&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But as time goes by, it&rsquo;s becoming clearer that CES isn&rsquo;t serving anyone very well. Not the public, not the press, not even the manufacturers. And that is down to four reasons.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;First, the elephant in the room: When 2,700 companies try to introduce new products simultaneously, they&rsquo;re guaranteed to dilute the press coverage. In fact, a CES debut is the worst way to make a product announcement - you&rsquo;ll be lost in the shuffle. Why not wait for a quieter week in tech, when your news has some impact?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;(I can tell you firsthand how ineffective CES announcements are. In the month of December 2012, I received over 300 email pitches for products coming out at CES 2013. I write &lt;a href=\"http://pogue.blogs.nytimes.com/\"&gt;two columns a week for the &lt;em&gt;New York Times&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. You do the math.)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;iPad clones&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The second worrisome sign: the big names are leaving the show. Apple hasn&rsquo;t attended CES for years, preferring to announce new products when they&rsquo;re ready, rather than being locked to CES&rsquo;s annual January cycle. Dell&rsquo;s stopped renting booths in 1997; like Lenovo (the world&rsquo;s number two computer maker), Dell now sends reps only for hotel-room meetings.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And this year another major firm will be missing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For over a decade, Microsoft not only brought a booth the size of Britain, but CEO Bill Gates or Steve Ballmer gave the opening-night keynote speech. But no longer. &ldquo;Our product news milestones generally don&rsquo;t align with the show&rsquo;s January timing,&rdquo; Microsoft said in a statement.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;(And what consumer-electronics megabrand will be replacing Microsoft for this year&rsquo;s opening CES keynote speech? Qualcomm, a maker of cellphone circuits. Hold on to your hats!)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Microsoft&rsquo;s departure doesn&rsquo;t mean the end of CES, but other companies may well follow suit.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Veteran tech reporters may remember that the biggest tech show &lt;em&gt;used &lt;/em&gt;to be Comdex, the computer show also held in Las Vegas. But when IBM pulled out in 1998, Comdex began a long, slow decline. By 2004, Comdex was history.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A third problem: the timing is horrible. Everybody knows that in the gadget biz, you want to unveil your shiny new product just in time for the holiday shopping season. But January? You&rsquo;ve barely formed your strategy, let alone polished up the new gizmos for the following Christmas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;No wonder so many new products introduced at CES never actually make it to store shelves. Companies are forced to pre-announce unfinished goods&mdash;and tip off their competition in the process.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Finally, CES has become a platform for electronics trade groups to push what they hope will become new, exciting trends. Sometimes, these trends catch on; the VCR (1970), consumer camcorder (1981), and the DVD (1996) all debuted at CES shows.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But lately, the industry has been pushing technology nobody wants. The 2013 show will be the seventh consecutive edition where we&rsquo;re told we&rsquo;ll surf the Web on our TV sets, and the fourth where we&rsquo;re told we want to put on 3D glasses to watch TV, and the third where cheap Chinese iPad clones will flood the show floor. Year after year, the public has spoken: no matter how much you shove these technologies in our faces, we&rsquo;re not interested.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So what is CES? A very expensive tradeshow that&rsquo;s at the wrong time of the year, attended by fewer important companies, trying to set an agenda the public doesn&rsquo;t want, by throwing 2,700 press releases at journalists who have room for only a few stories.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Here&rsquo;s one more safe prediction: In the tech business, the only constant is change. If CES can&rsquo;t justify its existence soon, it could be the next Comdex.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with David?&lt;/em&gt; I&lt;em&gt;f you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","HeadlineLong":"CES: Does the consumer technology show have a future?","HeadlineShort":"Powering down?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"This week's Consumer Electronics Show (CES) in Las Vegas is one of the world's largest showcases for new gadgets and technology. But CES veteran David Pogue wonders for how long.\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":" This week's Consumer Electronics Show in Las Vegas is one of the world's largest showcases for new gadgets. But CES veteran David Pogue wonders for how long.","SummaryShort":"Does the CES gadget show have a future?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"dcc77271-c44c-4bb4-8f0e-3440d8ada608","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130104-does-ces-have-a-future","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130104-does-ces-have-a-future"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130104-does-ces-have-a-future","_id":"559a6a1982f2d2dbf7bd4859"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"tomorrows_world.html","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"infographic","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":null,"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;They range from the serious to the fanciful, from the exciting to the petrifying.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And to get a gauge on how likely they are to happen, we asked the special bets department at British betting firm Ladbrokes to give us their odds on each prediction coming true.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;What have we missed? What else would you have liked to have seen in this graphic? Let us know&lt;em&gt; on our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:12:48Z","HeadlineLong":"Tomorrows world: A guide to the next 150 years","HeadlineShort":"Tomorrow's world","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"As we begin a new year, BBC Future has compiled 40 intriguing predictions made by scientists, politicians, journalists, bloggers and other assorted pundits in recent years about the shape of the world from 2013 to 2150. \n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"infographic","SummaryLong":"Explore our graphical guide to the advances of the future, from 2013 to 2150.","SummaryShort":"What do the next 150 years hold?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:48Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"8de9a6ae-7826-4fd0-9495-2d32c56f7905","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130102-tomorrows-world","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:48Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130102-tomorrows-world"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130102-tomorrows-world","_id":"559a6a1982f2d2dbf7bd4855"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Another New Year, another list of resolutions to make&hellip; and no doubt break. For some, 2013 may begin by going to the gym, or giving up smoking. But what about a more thorough and personal approach towards self-improvement, like monitoring your thoughts, e-mails, or even your bowel movements? If the answer is yes, then perhaps this is the year that you&rsquo;ll be joining a growing group of people who are using technology to measure their lives in extraordinary detail.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Probably since the dawn of humanity, people have been fascinated by even the most minute details of their lives, and kept track of what was going on in their bodies and minds. The Roman philosopher Seneca tracked the food he ate and what he dreamt at night. Benjamin Franklin consistently recorded his performance on 13 measures, such as cleanliness, frugality and overindulgence, believing it would keep him virtuous. Engineer and architect Buckminster Fuller nicknamed himself &ldquo;guinea pig b&rdquo; and kept a diary on his daily life and ideas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But in the past, record-keeping was time-consuming, requiring a commitment that only a very few had the patience to muster. Others cared, but not THAT much. Today, that&rsquo;s changing, as it becomes easier to track everything, from diet to mood to sleep quality. Smartphones come equipped with features like GPS, accelerometers and gyroscopes that can record your activity, location and other vital statistics. Millions of fitness-focused or diet-conscious people track their workouts or their desserts with apps and devices like &lt;a href=\"http://www.fitbit.com/\"&gt;Fitbit&lt;/a&gt; that track physical activity or calories burned. New technology also makes it easier to share results with others &ndash; who doesn&rsquo;t want to brag the first time they run more than 5 miles (8km), or be praised when they&rsquo;ve managed to lose 10 pounds (5kg)?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A growing band of devotees centered in the United States, called the &lt;a href=\"http://quantifiedself.com/\"&gt;Quantified Self movement&lt;/a&gt;, has taken the effort to another level. Its members track a whole list of measures and push the technology available to keep tabs on their ideas, their mental health, even their microbiome &ndash; the colonies of bacteria that live on or inside their bodies. Some might scoff at their efforts to keep spreadsheets documenting their mood, diet or sleep patterns. &ldquo;But the insights that we could learn from having all this quantified self data available are almost unfathomable,&rdquo; says Amy Robinson, an organiser of Quantified Self Boston, a group that includes about 650 members and holds bimonthly meet-ups to share ideas and progress.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Quantified self helps you keep a reference to how you lived not only the day before but two weeks before or a year before,&rdquo; Robinson says. &ldquo;You know when you achieve your best, you know when you exceed your best and it makes that possible by simply having the numbers there.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet such lofty personal goals come down to something that couldn&rsquo;t be more mundane: manually recording the number of daily bowel movements or every bite of food consumed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Lawrence David spent a year tracking 300 measures about his health and behaviour &ndash; including the microbes in his digestive system &ndash;&nbsp;while he was a graduate student at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. David created a self-tracking system by tinkering with an iPhone app designed to record and manage business cards. Every meal became a new &ldquo;card&rdquo; entry, with hundreds he had to type in by hand over the year &ndash; one night&rsquo;s entry could be cheeseburger and fries, the next pepperoni pizza. &ldquo;One of the few datasets out there is going to be full of junk food,&rdquo; he says, confessing to a personal weakness.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He got to the point where he hated the process so much that he didn&rsquo;t want to eat because then he&rsquo;d have to record it. &ldquo;It was terrible,&rdquo; he admits. &ldquo;Super tedious.&rdquo; But when his boss, associate professor Eric Alm, got food poisoning while self-tracking, it was the first time the microbial pattern of human diarrhoea had been followed in real-time. Sounds gross, but understanding the progress of food poisoning in such a manner could be an important step toward developing treatments for it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And other than confirming his love of junk food, he did learn some useful things about himself. David discovered that he wasted a lot of time on e-mail during the day, and wasn&rsquo;t as productive as he thought he was late at night. Now, he saves most of his e-mailing for the evenings &ndash; instead of disrupting his research to respond &ndash; and doesn&rsquo;t feel guilty when he takes a night off. He&rsquo;s stopped tracking most of the other measures, though.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Number power&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Like David, others who use technology to self-track rely on a mix of mind-numbing and automated measures, and they talk about the greater good, as well as what they&rsquo;ve learned about themselves. The Quantified Self movement originated in San Francisco, after it was first proposed by Wired editors Gary Wolf and Kevin Kelly in 2007. As of October 2012, the Quantified Self movement counted more than 70 meet-up groups worldwide with over 5,000 members &ndash; of course, they keep track of that sort of thing. (There&rsquo;s actually a greater number of self-tracking apps on mobile phones than members &ndash; around 7,500 at the moment.) Many of these self-trackers are researchers, computer scientists, or simply tech-savvy, 21st-century Benjamin Franklins who think the act of collecting so much data has the power to transform themselves and the rest of society.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Meet-ups are part 7-minute show-and-tells, part social hour. At her first meet-up, in Alabama, Amy Robinson was the only one who had tried quantifying anything, while other attendees were just curious about the idea. In Boston, as you might expect from being an academic and tech hub, many of the participants have developed their own hacks and want to trade tips on how to maximise their record-keeping.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Robinson says she has long been fascinated with where ideas come from, so she started sending herself an e-mail whenever a good one popped into her head. Recently, she collected &lt;a href=\"http://joost.1.ai/amy/\"&gt;six months of these e-mails&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; 770 in all &ndash; analysed them, and presented the results at a Quantified Self meetup in Boston in October. Among the themes that came up most often were &ldquo;science&rdquo;, &ldquo;beautiful&rdquo; and &ldquo;health.&rdquo; She found that she had the most new ideas immediately after attending an inspiring conference, travelling or experiencing something new. Doing this exercise helped feed her fascination with the human mind, she told the group, as well as &ldquo;discover more ways to think about myself and discover how my mind works relative to other people&rsquo;s.&rdquo;&lt;span&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/span&gt;Melanie Swan, founder of DIYgenomics, a California-based non-profit that promotes personalised medicine, has presented her findings at several Bay Area meet-ups, and she says meet-up attendees have changed as the movement has evolved. At first, the typical person was obsessive, &ldquo;someone who is really rigorous about self-tracking either because of a health reason or they&rsquo;re compelled to improve their mental performance or whatever,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;But who&rsquo;s now at the meet-ups is a much broader slice, though I would say it&rsquo;s still a pretty tech-forward thinking kind of audience &ndash; anybody interested in improving an aspect of their health.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Power of the nudge&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While their interest in self-quantification is technological, another factor is a more general one: the growing obsession with self-improvement over the past few decades, says Natasha Schull, a cultural anthropologist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology.&lt;strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;Weight Watchers has been telling people since the 1970s that if they want to lose weight, they need to track what they eat. The field of behavioural economics,&nbsp;which studies the connection between thoughts and financial decisions, has found that if you alter peoples&rsquo; social environment, they might be nudged into conducting more rational behaviour. For instance, if school cafeterias display fruit before fried chicken, students are more likely to eat fruit because they grab the first food they see. And having people track their actions makes a difference, says Schull, encouraging more people to make the effort to track.&lt;strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;Self-tracking might not have been such a fringe activity in the past, either. There were plenty of people keeping diaries and self-accounts in the 1800s and 1900s, says Schull. &ldquo;Even having a watch and being mindful of time is a form of self-regulation that went along with industrialisation,&rdquo; she says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As long as there have been people tracking themselves, there have been people mocking those trackers, Schull adds. D.H. Lawrence &lt;a href=\"http://www2.sunysuffolk.edu/lewiss/lawrence.htm\"&gt;wrote&lt;/a&gt; a piece making fun of Franklin&rsquo;s self-obsession and the cockiness embedded in his 13 virtues, including this description: &ldquo;He was a little model, was Benjamin. Doctor Franklin. Snuff-coloured little man! Immortal soul and all!&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Behind such insults lies the idea that tracking the minutiae of one&rsquo;s life can be pointless, an end in itself. Others worry that it could be used for self-destructive purposes, like an anorexic tracking how little she eats. &ldquo;It seems to me in the contemporary US example, the means and the end are blurring,&rdquo; says Schull, who co-taught a class at Harvard University in the autumn called &ldquo;Self as Data&rdquo;. &ldquo;I think people are still motivated &ndash; at least they say they are &ndash; by some kind of transformation, but it&rsquo;s seen as something that doesn&rsquo;t really end.&rdquo; Like the Alcoholics Anonymous model, where people who were once alcoholics are always recovering, Schull says. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a different model of what health is.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Weight and see&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For Mark Gerstein, a professor of bioinformatics at Yale University, tracking his weight helped him keep it under control. &ldquo;If you watch things, you tend to do better, by virtue of being aware of them,&rdquo; says Gerstein, who has a wifi-enabled bathroom scale that automatically records his weight and feeds it into a database.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Gerstein has also been tracking his asthma symptoms in hope of being able to reduce his medication. He says he sees a little correlation between his exercise and his peak air flow, and thinks the more he can measure about himself, the more he&rsquo;ll know about what triggers his wheezing and the better he&rsquo;ll be at controlling it. It&rsquo;s not just Gerstein&rsquo;s data that&rsquo;s important, this kind of information from thousands of people who have asthma could become much more powerful when it&rsquo;s aggregated. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s a whole other level of what happens when all this information flows together and you can look for patterns and trends,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But not all self-quantification efforts are successful. Schull&rsquo;s student, Sneha Khullar, began &ndash; reluctantly &ndash; tracking several measures as a class assignment. In her final presentation to the class in December, she explained that she enjoyed using a pedometer to track her footsteps and an app called RescueTime, which showed she is most productive on Fridays &ndash; now that she&rsquo;s given up partying. But writing down everything she ate was too much of a drag, and piling up chocolate wrappers didn&rsquo;t help her moderate her sweet tooth.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to Khullar, a student at the Harvard Graduate School of Design, self-tracking needs to be easy enough to carry out routinely, but not so easy that it can be ignored. Having to convert miles into kilometres so she could figure out how much she had run required just enough effort to get her invested in the process, with an incentive to keep running more. &ldquo;I could not have imagined I could run more than 5 miles,&rdquo; she proudly told her classmates. But, these tools were way more successful in behaviour-enhancing than in controlling bad habits, she admitted.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And perhaps this barrier to participation is key to whether self-tracking will fully make the leap from tech obsessives to the mainstream public. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a question of when does it become easy and when does it become useful,&rdquo; says Stephen Wolfram, creator of the technical computing software Mathematica. He says he&rsquo;s been self-tracking for a quarter of a century &ndash; everything from his personal health to his e-mail to the use patterns of every room in his house. But there are some things even he declines to measure. He doesn&rsquo;t bother with skin conductivity measures, for instance, because he doesn&rsquo;t like wearing the devices. He doesn&rsquo;t wear a camera around his neck, &ldquo;because it&rsquo;s not socially realistic for the life I lead &ndash; maybe if I were much younger.&rdquo; And he doesn&rsquo;t record his side of phone conversations because speech recognition software still isn&rsquo;t good enough to provide an accurate transcript. Why would he want a transcript of every conversation? &ldquo;Am I repeating myself? You can immediately check,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Privacy matters&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The next crucial step for self-tracking, several people said, will be the ability to integrate different data streams. Right now, the wifi-enabled scale doesn&rsquo;t talk to the program that monitors blood pressure, diet or sleep &ndash; but such integration will come eventually. Maybe someday it will even link up with &ldquo;smart&rdquo; toilets that can read shifting microbial populations and warn of impending illness. The key will be finding ways to make the data meaningful, rather than just overwhelming. And to keep it private.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yale&rsquo;s Gerstein says he worries a lot about the privacy aspects of all thins data. Right now, it seems harmless to share information about our sleep patterns, genes and microbial populations. But one day, that information could be more meaningful &ndash; and could be used against us. Sleep patterns could be relevant, say, in a court case about a traffic accident. People who post genetic information now are revealing things about their future children and grandchildren. &ldquo;I think the fear is people will pile up a lot of information about themselves and share it without fully seeing the implications,&rdquo; Gerstein says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Robinson says there&rsquo;s no question the Quantified Self movement she is part of will have to address privacy issues. &ldquo;Let the people who want to share their data in a way that maintains their privacy, but also helps other people,&rdquo; she says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And doctors are going to need to learn how to deal with all the data their patients are beginning to bring them. Right now, Robinson says, people in the Quantified Self movement &ldquo;want to share their information with their doctor, but their doctor doesn&rsquo;t really know what to do with it.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Wolfram, the founder and CEO of &lt;a href=\"http://www.wolfram.com/\"&gt;Wolfram Research&lt;/a&gt; and founder of the technology company Wolfram|Alpha, thinks the data will eventually redefine what doctors do. A doctor would be hard-pressed to make sense of the data once people are wired to track their heart rate, blood sugar levels, blood pressure and dozens of other measures. But computers will be able to easily spot patterns and trends, diagnosing far more accurately than doctors can today, and far earlier in the disease process, says Wolfram, who is developing analytics to meet this need. He thinks that eventually we&rsquo;ll go from sensor-based data to being able to treat whatever concern the technology diagnoses.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If so, perhaps a Quantified Selfer will figure out a way to measure whether this actually improves our health.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"http://anonymouse.org/cgi-bin/anon-www.cgi/https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"http://anonymouse.org/cgi-bin/anon-www.cgi/https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-01-03T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Quantified self: The tech-based route to a better life?","HeadlineShort":"A measured life","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Self-improvement has never been easier, thanks to apps and devices that measure all your everyday activities. Pogue writes a weekly &lt;a href=\"http://pogue.blogs.nytimes.com/\"&gt;tech column\nfor the New York Times&lt;/a&gt; and a monthly one for &lt;a href=\"http://www.scientificamerican.com/author.cfm?id=2544\"&gt;Scientific American&lt;/a&gt;.\nHe's the host of &lt;a href=\"http://www.pbs.org/wgbh/nova/sciencenow/\"&gt;NOVA\nScienceNow&lt;/a&gt;, other science shows on PBS, and is a correspondent\nfor &lt;a href=\"http://www.cbsnews.com/sunday-morning/\"&gt;CBS Sunday Morning&lt;/a&gt;. He is also one of the\nworld's bestselling how-to authors, with more than 3m books in print. You can find\nout more at his &lt;a href=\"http://www.davidpogue.com/index.html\"&gt;website&lt;/a&gt; or\nfollow him on Twitter at &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/Pogue\"&gt;@Pogue&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"David Pogue","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:11:45Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"bba24403-afd8-47bf-b88c-75fe7ddd77ad","Id":"wwfuture/author/david-pogue","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:11:45Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"david-pogue"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/david-pogue","_id":"559a668b82f2d2dbf7bcb5e8"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s a golden rule in consumer technology: you can&rsquo;t predict the future. Just ask the Popular Mechanics writer who said, in 1949, &ldquo;Computers in the future may weigh no more than 1.5 tons.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But 2013 presents a unique opportunity to make safe predictions. Maybe not about consumer tech, but at least about the annual trade show that celebrates it: the Consumer Electronics Show.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For years, CES was North America&rsquo;s biggest trade show. It&rsquo;s been running in various forms since 1967. Every January, nearly 150,000 attendees walk its 2,700 booths, then wait 45 minutes for taxis so they can return to their overpriced Las Vegas hotel rooms with aching feet.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So what&rsquo;s the prediction? That CES has started a long, slow decline.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;CES is not open to the public. It&rsquo;s attended exclusively by electronics representatives, investors, and members of the media. Its function: for tech companies to display the new gadgets they&rsquo;ve been working on - and to beg the press to write about them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And we do. Every January, we, the press, dutifully report the industry&rsquo;s latest message. This will be the year we surf the web on our TV sets! This will be the year for 3D television! This will be the year someone besides Apple has a hit tablet!&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But as time goes by, it&rsquo;s becoming clearer that CES isn&rsquo;t serving anyone very well. Not the public, not the press, not even the manufacturers. And that is down to four reasons.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;First, the elephant in the room: When 2,700 companies try to introduce new products simultaneously, they&rsquo;re guaranteed to dilute the press coverage. In fact, a CES debut is the worst way to make a product announcement - you&rsquo;ll be lost in the shuffle. Why not wait for a quieter week in tech, when your news has some impact?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;(I can tell you firsthand how ineffective CES announcements are. In the month of December 2012, I received over 300 email pitches for products coming out at CES 2013. I write &lt;a href=\"http://pogue.blogs.nytimes.com/\"&gt;two columns a week for the &lt;em&gt;New York Times&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. You do the math.)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;iPad clones&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The second worrisome sign: the big names are leaving the show. Apple hasn&rsquo;t attended CES for years, preferring to announce new products when they&rsquo;re ready, rather than being locked to CES&rsquo;s annual January cycle. Dell&rsquo;s stopped renting booths in 1997; like Lenovo (the world&rsquo;s number two computer maker), Dell now sends reps only for hotel-room meetings.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And this year another major firm will be missing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For over a decade, Microsoft not only brought a booth the size of Britain, but CEO Bill Gates or Steve Ballmer gave the opening-night keynote speech. But no longer. &ldquo;Our product news milestones generally don&rsquo;t align with the show&rsquo;s January timing,&rdquo; Microsoft said in a statement.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;(And what consumer-electronics megabrand will be replacing Microsoft for this year&rsquo;s opening CES keynote speech? Qualcomm, a maker of cellphone circuits. Hold on to your hats!)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Microsoft&rsquo;s departure doesn&rsquo;t mean the end of CES, but other companies may well follow suit.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Veteran tech reporters may remember that the biggest tech show &lt;em&gt;used &lt;/em&gt;to be Comdex, the computer show also held in Las Vegas. But when IBM pulled out in 1998, Comdex began a long, slow decline. By 2004, Comdex was history.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A third problem: the timing is horrible. Everybody knows that in the gadget biz, you want to unveil your shiny new product just in time for the holiday shopping season. But January? You&rsquo;ve barely formed your strategy, let alone polished up the new gizmos for the following Christmas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;No wonder so many new products introduced at CES never actually make it to store shelves. Companies are forced to pre-announce unfinished goods&mdash;and tip off their competition in the process.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Finally, CES has become a platform for electronics trade groups to push what they hope will become new, exciting trends. Sometimes, these trends catch on; the VCR (1970), consumer camcorder (1981), and the DVD (1996) all debuted at CES shows.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But lately, the industry has been pushing technology nobody wants. The 2013 show will be the seventh consecutive edition where we&rsquo;re told we&rsquo;ll surf the Web on our TV sets, and the fourth where we&rsquo;re told we want to put on 3D glasses to watch TV, and the third where cheap Chinese iPad clones will flood the show floor. Year after year, the public has spoken: no matter how much you shove these technologies in our faces, we&rsquo;re not interested.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So what is CES? A very expensive tradeshow that&rsquo;s at the wrong time of the year, attended by fewer important companies, trying to set an agenda the public doesn&rsquo;t want, by throwing 2,700 press releases at journalists who have room for only a few stories.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Here&rsquo;s one more safe prediction: In the tech business, the only constant is change. If CES can&rsquo;t justify its existence soon, it could be the next Comdex.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with David?&lt;/em&gt; I&lt;em&gt;f you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a66a082f2d2dbf7bcb96f"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","HeadlineLong":"CES: Does the consumer technology show have a future?","HeadlineShort":"Powering down?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"This week's Consumer Electronics Show (CES) in Las Vegas is one of the world's largest showcases for new gadgets and technology.
