the recent Mobile World Congress in Barcelona there was an unspoken truth: all the phones looked the same.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But, as Marc Cieslak discovers, it is what is inside that counts.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;You can also find the Click team on &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCClick\" target=\"_self\"&gt;Facebook&lt;/a&gt; or at &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBCCLICK\" target=\"_self\"&gt;@BBCClick&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","HeadlineLong":"Why do smartphones all look the same?","HeadlineShort":"Why do phones all look the same?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Sleek. Slim. Glossy. Packing apps and a touchscreen.\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":[],"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"video","SummaryLong":"Sleek. Slim. Glossy. Packing apps and a touchscreen. Phones increasingly look the same. But, as Marc Cieslak discovers, it is what is inside that counts.","SummaryShort":"And is there anything that differentiates the line-up of modern handsets?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"1dde7bdb-4ca9-4a0b-8191-46cbdc018c3a","Id":"wwfuture/story/20120305-why-do-phones-all-look-the-same","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20120305-why-do-phones-all-look-the-same"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20120305-why-do-phones-all-look-the-same","_id":"559a69fd82f2d2dbf7bd44dd"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;When the head of Apple strode on to stage at the California Theater on Tuesday evening the expectant crowd whooped and cheered. It was a similar &ndash; albeit more low key &ndash; reception at Pier 57 in New York City on Thursday, when the head of Microsoft appeared.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The tail end of October has been dominated by the Big Two launches: &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/technology-20042738\"&gt;Apple&rsquo;s iPad Mini&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/technology-20072497\"&gt;Microsoft&rsquo; Windows 8 and Surface tablet&lt;/a&gt;. Complete with banked photographers and ecstatic press scrums trying to get a glimpse of the latest &ldquo;model&rdquo;, they felt as much like fashion shows as product launches.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But that should come as no surprise. It is all part of a shift in technology that has gradually moved away from an emphasis on utility to today&rsquo;s holy trinity of look, feel and lifestyle.&nbsp; A shift pioneered by Apple but increasingly championed by all tech firms, it takes its cues from fashion, positioning tablets, computers and software as cultural beacons: stamps that immediately say who you are or, rather, who you aspire to be. If anything proves just how far technology is ingrained in our lives, it is this.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet what&rsquo;s most significant about this move is not what it tells us about Apple or Microsoft or any other technology firm, but what it tells us about ourselves.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Everyone think different&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For most people, a modern tablet computer or operating system is a minor miracle of excess, able comfortably to accomplish every daily digital task demanded of it. But what we merely do is no longer the key factor. Far more significant is the way that owning and using these objects makes us feel: status, self-expression and how we would like to be perceived by others.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It is something that is subtly and repeatedly emphasised by tech firms. The rhetoric around the iPad mini is familiar enough, with &lt;a href=\"http://www.apple.com/ipad-mini/overview/\"&gt;official descriptions&lt;/a&gt; emphasizing its beauty, fluidity and tactile intimacy of experience. Even Microsoft, though, are now playing the same game for all it's worth, with the public presentation of Windows 8 coming across as an attempt to out-Apple Apple.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to Microsoft&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://go.microsoft.com/fwlink/?LinkId=251529\"&gt;product guide&lt;/a&gt;, &ldquo;Windows has been reimagined to focus on your life. The beautiful, fast, and fluid design is perfect for a range of hardware... it&rsquo;s smooth, intuitive, and gives you instant access...&rdquo; And so on. What&rsquo;s on offer is a kind of technological sublime, promising not only the ultimate lifestyle accessory, but a place where the experience of living itself can be perfected.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The move from technology manufacturer to fashion label has helped Apple become the world&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-19325913\"&gt;most valuable company&lt;/a&gt;. More than anyone, it has defined the increasingly image-conscious space within which digital technologies try to position themselves. Think of its &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1984_%28advertisement%29\"&gt;1984 advert&lt;/a&gt; for the first Macintosh or its &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Think_Different\"&gt;&ldquo;Think Different&rdquo; campaign of 1997&lt;/a&gt;, which emphasised that buying into a technology immediately bought you membership of a cultural elite. This worked well, but what really gave it a kick was when gadgets left the office and appeared on the high street. With mobile phones and MP3 players, people were able to show off just how different they were all the time by simply popping a million identical pairs of white headphones into their ear.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Today, Apple had completed its transition from a technology company into a lifestyle brand: something that comes complete with apparent media privileges. As Alexis Madrigal, editor of the Atlantic tech &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/alexismadrigal/status/260805815718526978\"&gt;noted&lt;/a&gt; when Apple showed its latest commercial: &ldquo;Gotta say: that's a damn good iPad Mini commercial. Luckily, the press does all the work of explaining the thing, so Apple can just brand.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The conveniences of a compliant technology press are not to be underestimated. But there is a still more significant aspect to being a lifestyle brand within the digital space, closely related to something I call emotional obsolescence: the point at which a purchase stops imparting the gratification it first afforded.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The promise that novelty and beauty can bring happiness and status is hardly a new feature of consumerism. Yet this sentiment, imported from the realms of brands and fashion, has arrived among digital technologies in a particularly intense &ndash; and intensely lucrative &ndash; form.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Never satisfied&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As the author (and fellow BBC Future columnist) Matt Novak &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/paleofuture/status/260802228284166144\"&gt;noted on Twitter&lt;/a&gt; when Apple introduced its fourth generation iPad on Tuesday: &ldquo;the real story here is that Apple shortened its planned obsolescence cycle to 6 months.&rdquo; Aiming to achieve a revolutionary technical breakthrough several times a year is an impossible business plan. Fuelling a frenzy of public feeling at the same interval, however, is eminently possible.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hence the heady ecstasy of the modern product launch &ndash; and the fact that the best generator of technological profit margins in 2012 isn&rsquo;t features or value for money, but the very fact that there is another model out there which is newer and different. It is an emotional experience that money most certainly can buy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s also a seductive, dangerous game to play. Can one company continue to dominate perceptions of quality, cool and desire? Microsoft, Samsung, Google and many others are betting not. Will consumers continue to buy into shorter lifespans and incremental changes to products? Only time will tell.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To my mind, though, what ultimately differentiates digital technologies from fashion and other forms of consumption is the nature of the promise being sold &ndash; and of the emotional obsolescence being rammed down purchasers&rsquo; throats. The notion of shoes and clothes making you a better person is one kind of fiction, and a powerful one at that. But with interactive media, what&rsquo;s being promised is a lifestyle in a far more literal sense: a complete system for expressing yourself in the world, not to mention regularly topping up the premium status of this existence with purchases.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Similarly, an operating system that &ldquo;works the way you do&rdquo; &ndash; as Microsoft &lt;a href=\"http://go.microsoft.com/fwlink/?LinkId=251529\"&gt;puts it&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; makes a powerful appeal to our sense of self. It may also offer a magnificent user experience. Yet it begs the question: if this is so great, why should I keep on seeking something better?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One answer is that this seeking is just another part of how we work: never satisfied, seeking perfection in continual motion away from the present. And while this isn&rsquo;t the whole story, it is a large part of the vision of lifestyle and humanity that the freshest crop of product launches are selling.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There&rsquo;s an important warning in all this. As MIT professor Sherry Turkle puts it, in her recent book &lt;a href=\"http://noetic.org/noetic/issue-eight-march/alone-together-why-we-expect-more-from-technology/\"&gt;Alone Together&lt;/a&gt;, &ldquo;Technology&nbsp;is seductive when what it offers&nbsp;meets&nbsp;our human vulnerabilities.&rdquo; More than ever, we need to make sure we know what we're really signing up for when we plug into these new seductive lifestyles. Buy and enjoy by all means. But remember: few things are more perfectly engineered to fade over time than seduction.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with Tom? If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","HeadlineLong":"Apples fashionable seduction","HeadlineShort":"Apples fashionable seduction","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The iPad maker is leading a subtle change in the tech industry which has one simple goal: getting consumers to buy more. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"The iPad maker is leading a subtle change in the tech industry which has one simple goal: getting consumers to buy more.","SummaryShort":"How the tech firm keeps people buying","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"a4eea8c6-de97-4b9c-8a92-d8480ca76252","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121026-apples-fashionable-seduction","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121026-apples-fashionable-seduction"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121026-apples-fashionable-seduction","_id":"559a6a1282f2d2dbf7bd4779"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Another New Year, another list of resolutions to make&hellip; and no doubt break. For some, 2013 may begin by going to the gym, or giving up smoking. But what about a more thorough and personal approach towards self-improvement, like monitoring your thoughts, e-mails, or even your bowel movements? If the answer is yes, then perhaps this is the year that you&rsquo;ll be joining a growing group of people who are using technology to measure their lives in extraordinary detail.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Probably since the dawn of humanity, people have been fascinated by even the most minute details of their lives, and kept track of what was going on in their bodies and minds. The Roman philosopher Seneca tracked the food he ate and what he dreamt at night. Benjamin Franklin consistently recorded his performance on 13 measures, such as cleanliness, frugality and overindulgence, believing it would keep him virtuous. Engineer and architect Buckminster Fuller nicknamed himself &ldquo;guinea pig b&rdquo; and kept a diary on his daily life and ideas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But in the past, record-keeping was time-consuming, requiring a commitment that only a very few had the patience to muster. Others cared, but not THAT much. Today, that&rsquo;s changing, as it becomes easier to track everything, from diet to mood to sleep quality. Smartphones come equipped with features like GPS, accelerometers and gyroscopes that can record your activity, location and other vital statistics. Millions of fitness-focused or diet-conscious people track their workouts or their desserts with apps and devices like &lt;a href=\"http://www.fitbit.com/\"&gt;Fitbit&lt;/a&gt; that track physical activity or calories burned. New technology also makes it easier to share results with others &ndash; who doesn&rsquo;t want to brag the first time they run more than 5 miles (8km), or be praised when they&rsquo;ve managed to lose 10 pounds (5kg)?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A growing band of devotees centered in the United States, called the &lt;a href=\"http://quantifiedself.com/\"&gt;Quantified Self movement&lt;/a&gt;, has taken the effort to another level. Its members track a whole list of measures and push the technology available to keep tabs on their ideas, their mental health, even their microbiome &ndash; the colonies of bacteria that live on or inside their bodies. Some might scoff at their efforts to keep spreadsheets documenting their mood, diet or sleep patterns. &ldquo;But the insights that we could learn from having all this quantified self data available are almost unfathomable,&rdquo; says Amy Robinson, an organiser of Quantified Self Boston, a group that includes about 650 members and holds bimonthly meet-ups to share ideas and progress.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Quantified self helps you keep a reference to how you lived not only the day before but two weeks before or a year before,&rdquo; Robinson says. &ldquo;You know when you achieve your best, you know when you exceed your best and it makes that possible by simply having the numbers there.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet such lofty personal goals come down to something that couldn&rsquo;t be more mundane: manually recording the number of daily bowel movements or every bite of food consumed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Lawrence David spent a year tracking 300 measures about his health and behaviour &ndash; including the microbes in his digestive system &ndash;&nbsp;while he was a graduate student at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. David created a self-tracking system by tinkering with an iPhone app designed to record and manage business cards. Every meal became a new &ldquo;card&rdquo; entry, with hundreds he had to type in by hand over the year &ndash; one night&rsquo;s entry could be cheeseburger and fries, the next pepperoni pizza. &ldquo;One of the few datasets out there is going to be full of junk food,&rdquo; he says, confessing to a personal weakness.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He got to the point where he hated the process so much that he didn&rsquo;t want to eat because then he&rsquo;d have to record it. &ldquo;It was terrible,&rdquo; he admits. &ldquo;Super tedious.&rdquo; But when his boss, associate professor Eric Alm, got food poisoning while self-tracking, it was the first time the microbial pattern of human diarrhoea had been followed in real-time. Sounds gross, but understanding the progress of food poisoning in such a manner could be an important step toward developing treatments for it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And other than confirming his love of junk food, he did learn some useful things about himself. David discovered that he wasted a lot of time on e-mail during the day, and wasn&rsquo;t as productive as he thought he was late at night. Now, he saves most of his e-mailing for the evenings &ndash; instead of disrupting his research to respond &ndash; and doesn&rsquo;t feel guilty when he takes a night off. He&rsquo;s stopped tracking most of the other measures, though.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Number power&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Like David, others who use technology to self-track rely on a mix of mind-numbing and automated measures, and they talk about the greater good, as well as what they&rsquo;ve learned about themselves. The Quantified Self movement originated in San Francisco, after it was first proposed by Wired editors Gary Wolf and Kevin Kelly in 2007. As of October 2012, the Quantified Self movement counted more than 70 meet-up groups worldwide with over 5,000 members &ndash; of course, they keep track of that sort of thing. (There&rsquo;s actually a greater number of self-tracking apps on mobile phones than members &ndash; around 7,500 at the moment.) Many of these self-trackers are researchers, computer scientists, or simply tech-savvy, 21st-century Benjamin Franklins who think the act of collecting so much data has the power to transform themselves and the rest of society.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Meet-ups are part 7-minute show-and-tells, part social hour. At her first meet-up, in Alabama, Amy Robinson was the only one who had tried quantifying anything, while other attendees were just curious about the idea. In Boston, as you might expect from being an academic and tech hub, many of the participants have developed their own hacks and want to trade tips on how to maximise their record-keeping.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Robinson says she has long been fascinated with where ideas come from, so she started sending herself an e-mail whenever a good one popped into her head. Recently, she collected &lt;a href=\"http://joost.1.ai/amy/\"&gt;six months of these e-mails&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; 770 in all &ndash; analysed them, and presented the results at a Quantified Self meetup in Boston in October. Among the themes that came up most often were &ldquo;science&rdquo;, &ldquo;beautiful&rdquo; and &ldquo;health.&rdquo; She found that she had the most new ideas immediately after attending an inspiring conference, travelling or experiencing something new. Doing this exercise helped feed her fascination with the human mind, she told the group, as well as &ldquo;discover more ways to think about myself and discover how my mind works relative to other people&rsquo;s.&rdquo;&lt;span&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/span&gt;Melanie Swan, founder of DIYgenomics, a California-based non-profit that promotes personalised medicine, has presented her findings at several Bay Area meet-ups, and she says meet-up attendees have changed as the movement has evolved. At first, the typical person was obsessive, &ldquo;someone who is really rigorous about self-tracking either because of a health reason or they&rsquo;re compelled to improve their mental performance or whatever,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;But who&rsquo;s now at the meet-ups is a much broader slice, though I would say it&rsquo;s still a pretty tech-forward thinking kind of audience &ndash; anybody interested in improving an aspect of their health.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Power of the nudge&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While their interest in self-quantification is technological, another factor is a more general one: the growing obsession with self-improvement over the past few decades, says Natasha Schull, a cultural anthropologist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology.&lt;strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;Weight Watchers has been telling people since the 1970s that if they want to lose weight, they need to track what they eat. The field of behavioural economics,&nbsp;which studies the connection between thoughts and financial decisions, has found that if you alter peoples&rsquo; social environment, they might be nudged into conducting more rational behaviour. For instance, if school cafeterias display fruit before fried chicken, students are more likely to eat fruit because they grab the first food they see. And having people track their actions makes a difference, says Schull, encouraging more people to make the effort to track.&lt;strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;Self-tracking might not have been such a fringe activity in the past, either. There were plenty of people keeping diaries and self-accounts in the 1800s and 1900s, says Schull. &ldquo;Even having a watch and being mindful of time is a form of self-regulation that went along with industrialisation,&rdquo; she says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As long as there have been people tracking themselves, there have been people mocking those trackers, Schull adds. D.H. Lawrence &lt;a href=\"http://www2.sunysuffolk.edu/lewiss/lawrence.htm\"&gt;wrote&lt;/a&gt; a piece making fun of Franklin&rsquo;s self-obsession and the cockiness embedded in his 13 virtues, including this description: &ldquo;He was a little model, was Benjamin. Doctor Franklin. Snuff-coloured little man! Immortal soul and all!&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Behind such insults lies the idea that tracking the minutiae of one&rsquo;s life can be pointless, an end in itself. Others worry that it could be used for self-destructive purposes, like an anorexic tracking how little she eats. &ldquo;It seems to me in the contemporary US example, the means and the end are blurring,&rdquo; says Schull, who co-taught a class at Harvard University in the autumn called &ldquo;Self as Data&rdquo;. &ldquo;I think people are still motivated &ndash; at least they say they are &ndash; by some kind of transformation, but it&rsquo;s seen as something that doesn&rsquo;t really end.&rdquo; Like the Alcoholics Anonymous model, where people who were once alcoholics are always recovering, Schull says. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a different model of what health is.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Weight and see&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For Mark Gerstein, a professor of bioinformatics at Yale University, tracking his weight helped him keep it under control. &ldquo;If you watch things, you tend to do better, by virtue of being aware of them,&rdquo; says Gerstein, who has a wifi-enabled bathroom scale that automatically records his weight and feeds it into a database.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Gerstein has also been tracking his asthma symptoms in hope of being able to reduce his medication. He says he sees a little correlation between his exercise and his peak air flow, and thinks the more he can measure about himself, the more he&rsquo;ll know about what triggers his wheezing and the better he&rsquo;ll be at controlling it. It&rsquo;s not just Gerstein&rsquo;s data that&rsquo;s important, this kind of information from thousands of people who have asthma could become much more powerful when it&rsquo;s aggregated. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s a whole other level of what happens when all this information flows together and you can look for patterns and trends,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But not all self-quantification efforts are successful. Schull&rsquo;s student, Sneha Khullar, began &ndash; reluctantly &ndash; tracking several measures as a class assignment. In her final presentation to the class in December, she explained that she enjoyed using a pedometer to track her footsteps and an app called RescueTime, which showed she is most productive on Fridays &ndash; now that she&rsquo;s given up partying. But writing down everything she ate was too much of a drag, and piling up chocolate wrappers didn&rsquo;t help her moderate her sweet tooth.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to Khullar, a student at the Harvard Graduate School of Design, self-tracking needs to be easy enough to carry out routinely, but not so easy that it can be ignored. Having to convert miles into kilometres so she could figure out how much she had run required just enough effort to get her invested in the process, with an incentive to keep running more. &ldquo;I could not have imagined I could run more than 5 miles,&rdquo; she proudly told her classmates. But, these tools were way more successful in behaviour-enhancing than in controlling bad habits, she admitted.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And perhaps this barrier to participation is key to whether self-tracking will fully make the leap from tech obsessives to the mainstream public. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a question of when does it become easy and when does it become useful,&rdquo; says Stephen Wolfram, creator of the technical computing software Mathematica. He says he&rsquo;s been self-tracking for a quarter of a century &ndash; everything from his personal health to his e-mail to the use patterns of every room in his house. But there are some things even he declines to measure. He doesn&rsquo;t bother with skin conductivity measures, for instance, because he doesn&rsquo;t like wearing the devices. He doesn&rsquo;t wear a camera around his neck, &ldquo;because it&rsquo;s not socially realistic for the life I lead &ndash; maybe if I were much younger.&rdquo; And he doesn&rsquo;t record his side of phone conversations because speech recognition software still isn&rsquo;t good enough to provide an accurate transcript. Why would he want a transcript of every conversation? &ldquo;Am I repeating myself? You can immediately check,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Privacy matters&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The next crucial step for self-tracking, several people said, will be the ability to integrate different data streams. Right now, the wifi-enabled scale doesn&rsquo;t talk to the program that monitors blood pressure, diet or sleep &ndash; but such integration will come eventually. Maybe someday it will even link up with &ldquo;smart&rdquo; toilets that can read shifting microbial populations and warn of impending illness. The key will be finding ways to make the data meaningful, rather than just overwhelming. And to keep it private.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yale&rsquo;s Gerstein says he worries a lot about the privacy aspects of all thins data. Right now, it seems harmless to share information about our sleep patterns, genes and microbial populations. But one day, that information could be more meaningful &ndash; and could be used against us. Sleep patterns could be relevant, say, in a court case about a traffic accident. People who post genetic information now are revealing things about their future children and grandchildren. &ldquo;I think the fear is people will pile up a lot of information about themselves and share it without fully seeing the implications,&rdquo; Gerstein says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Robinson says there&rsquo;s no question the Quantified Self movement she is part of will have to address privacy issues. &ldquo;Let the people who want to share their data in a way that maintains their privacy, but also helps other people,&rdquo; she says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And doctors are going to need to learn how to deal with all the data their patients are beginning to bring them. Right now, Robinson says, people in the Quantified Self movement &ldquo;want to share their information with their doctor, but their doctor doesn&rsquo;t really know what to do with it.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Wolfram, the founder and CEO of &lt;a href=\"http://www.wolfram.com/\"&gt;Wolfram Research&lt;/a&gt; and founder of the technology company Wolfram|Alpha, thinks the data will eventually redefine what doctors do. A doctor would be hard-pressed to make sense of the data once people are wired to track their heart rate, blood sugar levels, blood pressure and dozens of other measures. But computers will be able to easily spot patterns and trends, diagnosing far more accurately than doctors can today, and far earlier in the disease process, says Wolfram, who is developing analytics to meet this need. He thinks that eventually we&rsquo;ll go from sensor-based data to being able to treat whatever concern the technology diagnoses.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If so, perhaps a Quantified Selfer will figure out a way to measure whether this actually improves our health.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"http://anonymouse.org/cgi-bin/anon-www.cgi/https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"http://anonymouse.org/cgi-bin/anon-www.cgi/https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-01-03T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Quantified self: The tech-based route to a better life?","HeadlineShort":"A measured life","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Self-improvement has never been easier, thanks to apps and devices that measure all your everyday activities. opinion.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Along the way we had to discard sources that came up with predictions that no one else was backing or where people were unwilling to take a stand on an issue.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For example, we all thought the future of the euro would feature heavily in the list. a fighter plane prepares to take off from a naval carrier at sea, the pilot and deck crew go through a tightly choreographed series of hand signals to tell each other they are ready to launch. It ends with a final &ldquo;salute&rdquo; from the pilot to indicate that the aircraft is ready to be catapulted off the deck.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But when the X-47B, the US Navy&rsquo;s newest prototype combat aircraft, prepares for its first carrier launch early next year, there will be no salute.&nbsp; That&rsquo;s because there will also be no pilot. Instead, the X-47B will blink its wingtip navigation lights, a robotic nod to the human salute (and mimicking what the Navy does for night launches), before the catapult officer presses the launch button, and the robotic aircraft is flung off the front of the ship&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;After years of development, and recent land-based tests, the highly anticipated carrier flight for this stealthy, tailless, unmanned drone is imminent. &ldquo;It should be in early in 2013,&rdquo; says Carl Johnson, vice president and program manager at defence firm Northrop Grumman, which builds the X-47B. &ldquo;We have to coordinate ship schedules as well as all the other airspace issues.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The X-47B is a strike fighter-sized prototype drone developed as part of the United States Navy's UCAS-D (Unmanned Combat Air System Demonstration) programme, which aims to develop technologies necessary to field a combat drone on carriers. As a result, it has folding wings and is built for the rigors of sea life, including salt water, deck handling and of course take-off and landing from an aircraft carrier.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although the X-47B is a prototype, the Navy hopes to actually field operational unmanned combat aircraft on carriers by the end of the decade.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The unmanned &ldquo;flying wing&rdquo; aircraft, which takes some of its design cues from Northrop Grumman&rsquo;s B-2 stealth bomber, is supposed to demonstrate reconnaissance and strike capabilities&mdash;it has a full-sized weapons bay, although the prototype will not fly with weapons.&nbsp; And, unlike existing drones, which are usually remotely &ldquo;flown&rdquo; by pilots once in the air, the X-47B is designed to fly autonomously, with just the occasional click of a mouse from an operator to send it instructions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;It&rsquo;s a big deal, but it&rsquo;s an extension of something that was already happening,&rdquo; says Peter Singer, a senior fellow at the Brooking Institution in Washington, DC, and the author of Wired for War, a book on the military&rsquo;s robotics revolution.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Forward fire&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The craft was revealed in 2008 but is only now undergoing sea tests aboard the USS Harry S. Truman, including moving around on the carrier. Whilst this kind of trial may not sound remarkable, in some ways it&rsquo;s one of the more challenging steps toward proving that the X-47B, which weighs in at 20,000 kg (44,000 lb) and has a 20m (62 ft) wing span, is ready for flight.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Getting around on a crowded flight deck is difficult, says Johnson, because the aircraft must maneuver very close the edge of the carrier, sometimes pivoting so that it appears that half the airplane is hanging off the ship. &ldquo;The precision involved in doing that is very difficult with a pilot following directions from a person on the deck,&rdquo; says Johnson. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s very difficult to do that as well with an unmanned system.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As a result, the engineers have built a wireless remote control device that can be used to move the aircraft around the deck.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The X-47B has already been tested on land in conditions meant to mimic operations on a carrier deck, including a catapult launch, but operating on a real carrier crowded with people and equipment presents fresh challenges. &nbsp;For example, the X-47B must be tested for electromagnetic interference, in other words, making sure that the aircraft&rsquo;s electronic systems don&rsquo;t clash with the myriad radar and emitters that are on a ship.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;While we go through a rigorous test program, you really learn a lot when you&rsquo;re at sea and you&rsquo;re validating your system against the true environment of the carrier,&rdquo; says Johnson.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If all goes well with these tests, the Navy will then be ready for its first at-sea flight. This will likely be a short affair, according to Johnson, and will start with a catapult launch and end with the aircraft landing not on the carrier, but on firm ground. Later that year, the X-47B will also perform an &ldquo;arrested landing,&rdquo; meaning it will land back on the aircraft carrier.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another key flight test will take place in 2014, when the X-47 demonstrates that it can perform autonomous aerial refueling. Currently, the craft has a range of around 3,200km (2,000 miles) and can stay aloft for six hours. But for effective operations, the Navy would like it to stay aloft for longer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Head-to-head&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even if all those tests go smoothly, that doesn&rsquo;t mean the X-47B will actually be deployed. The stealthy, aircraft is still merely a prototype. The Navy soon plans to launch a new program to develop an operational unmanned combat aircraft, which will involve fielding up to half a dozen armed drones on carriers by the end of the decade as part of what&rsquo;s called the Unmanned Carrier-Launched Airborne Surveillance and Strike program.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Early next year, the Navy will hold a competition to build the new drone. That means Northrop, the incumbent, will have to compete against other companies, including Lockheed Martin, which built the stealthy RQ-170 (famously captured by Iran), and General Atomics, which makes the familiar Reaper and Predator drones, and Boeing, which developed the X-45, a one-time competitor to Northrop&rsquo;s drone.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even as a prototype, however, the X-47B&rsquo;s upcoming launch from an aircraft carrier, the &ldquo;heart of US naval aviation&rdquo;, marks a significant watershed for drones, says Singer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;It&rsquo;s one of the places where we haven&rsquo;t seen them yet.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ultimately, the X-47B&rsquo;s upcoming flight is not about proving that drones can work&mdash;that&rsquo;s already been done&mdash;but expanding how and where they are used. &ldquo;The Wright Brothers moment already happened,&rdquo; says Singer. &ldquo;Now we&rsquo;re in the equivalent of 1920s and 1930s.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook&lt;/a&gt; page or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","HeadlineLong":"X-47B stealth drone targets new frontiers","HeadlineShort":"Stealth drone hits new frontiers","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The US Navy's cutting-edge robot fighter plane aims to be the first unmanned aerial vehicle to take-off and land at sea.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"The US Navy's cutting-edge robot fighter plane aims to be the first unmanned aerial vehicle to take-off and land at sea.","SummaryShort":"Radical robot jet prepares for take off","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"d54edd3c-85a7-4f61-a9ba-f113bf3dbecd","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121218-stealth-drone-targets-life-at-sea","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121218-stealth-drone-targets-life-at-sea"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121218-stealth-drone-targets-life-at-sea","_id":"559a6a1882f2d2dbf7bd4827"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;The dangers of talking on a mobile phone or texting whilst driving are well known.&nbsp; So adding what, on first glance, look like added distractions may not seem like the best idea.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But researchers and car firms are increasingly looking to give drivers enhanced, or augmented reality (AR) that adds extra information ranging from directions to social media feeds to the view through the windscreen. And, perhaps the most unintuitive part of it is that it is designed to make the roads more secure for all of us.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The biggest way augmented reality can be useful is in supporting safe driving,&rdquo; says Professor Anind Dey, of the&nbsp;&lt;a href=\"http://www.hcii.cs.cmu.edu/\"&gt;Human-Computer Interaction (HCI) Institute&lt;/a&gt;&nbsp;at&nbsp;&lt;a href=\"http://www.cmu.edu/\"&gt;Carnegie Mellon University&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;The ability to enhance information around us that might otherwise be opaque to us is invaluable.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augmented_reality\"&gt;AR&lt;/a&gt; is usually considered to be a live view of the real world, onto which extra data &ndash; usually pulled from the internet - is layered or superimposed. It is similar to the view of the world that Arnold Schwarzenegger had in the Terminator movies. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The technology is already well established elsewhere. For example, there are numerous smartphone apps that allow users to overlay the view from their camera with everything from directions and transport options to restaurant reviews and house prices. Point your phone at something of interest, power up your app and you are away.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Companies like Google are taking it one step further. Earlier this &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/technology-17618495\"&gt;year the search giant unveiled its Google Glass project&lt;/a&gt;, a pair of minimal glasses that overlay a person&rsquo;s vision with everything from weather data, location and diary appointments. &nbsp;&nbsp;It is an idea that has been seized upon by other firms, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/technology-20462840\"&gt;such as Microsoft&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And now AR is making its way into cars. It builds on so-called &ldquo;heads up display&rdquo; (HUD) technology that is commonly used in fighter planes and began to make its way into high-end cars in the late 1990s. These HUDs are frequently used to project directions or speed onto the windscreen in the driver&rsquo;s line of sight to minimize the amount of time the driver takes their eyes off the road. But augmented reality could allow these displays to go much further than that.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Looking glass&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Car manufacturers such as BMW, Toyota, Mercedes and GM have shown off various prototypes in recent years. For example, a Mercedes concept unveiled earlier this year and known as Dice allowed drivers to conjure up information about passing places of interest by merely pointing at them while friends driving past the car show up as an icon on the windscreen, alongside their social network status.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another concept from GM, known as the enhanced vision system, uses an array of sensors and cameras mounted inside and outside the vehicle to monitor the environment around the car and the driver&rsquo;s eye and head movements. This information is then used to overlay the view from the windscreen with relevant information about driving conditions &ndash; such as the location of the building you are trying to find or dangers that you may not be aware of, such as an animal or child at the side of the road. Another mode marks the edges of the road when fog obscures the drivers view.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Other concepts take AR even further. Japanese car maker Toyota has shown off a prototype of &lt;a href=\"http://reviews.cnet.com/8301-13746_7-20081402-48/toyota-demos-augmented-reality-enhanced-car-windows/\"&gt;&ldquo;enhanced car windows&rdquo;&lt;/a&gt; that would allow passengers to zoom in on places and objects of interest that they are passing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, the most extreme concept comes from designers at &lt;a href=\"http://tachilab.org/modules/projects/rpt.html\"&gt;Keio University in Japan&lt;/a&gt;, who are working on technology that will make the back seat of a car appear transparent, so that when drivers are reversing they can see everything around them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Their experimental setup uses cameras mounted outside the car &lt;a href=\"http://tachilab.org/modules/projects/transparentcockpit.html\"&gt;to project an image inside the car in the driver&rsquo;s field of view&lt;/a&gt;. Using similar technology to a heads-up display, the image is shown on a reflective screen mounted behind and between the two front seats. When a driver looks back over his shoulder, he sees the &ldquo;real&rdquo; image out of the rear view window continued, through augmented reality, right down the seats to the floor.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Innovations like this may be a long way from going into production. But other uses of AR could appear much sooner. For example, the most common use of AR is to overlay the world with directions that are overlayed on the road in front of you. Almost all concepts show some variant of this and, earlier this year, electronics firm Pioneer claimed to introduce the &lt;a href=\"http://www.engadget.com/2012/05/09/pioneer-touts-worlds-first-car-gps-with-augmented-reality/\"&gt;world&rsquo;s first GPS with AR&lt;/a&gt;, that can be installed in any vehicle.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Game view&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This kind of innovation would be helpful to any driver. But Professor Dey and his team believe it could be especially useful for &lt;a href=\"http://www.cs.cmu.edu/%7Esjunikim/download/SJKimCHI09.pdf\"&gt;older people&lt;/a&gt;, whose sense of spatial awareness may be reduced. This is especially problematic when they must look down at a navigation system, for example.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The amount of time being spent not looking out the windshield, and the number of times that occurs on a short drive, can get pretty high,&rdquo; he explains. &ldquo;Particularly when you are talking about elder drivers who may have diminished facilities for driving, you want to reduce the amount of time they are looking away.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To test this theory, he devised a simulator that projects the navigation system across the full width of the screen. The driver sees the real world, but with a map superimposed on top. As turns or streets come up, the map updates like a panning computerised window that is locked to the real world around them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although the system is a prototype, it has given Prof Dey and his team clues about how a real system should work. For example, to ensure that one set of distractions is not replaced by another, he believes that drivers should be allowed to personalize a system so that they can receive notifications from the system in ways that feel intuitive to them, such as audio tones, or even by touch, using haptic feedback through the steering wheel and seats.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&nbsp;&ldquo;We should be using technologies like augmented reality to personalise the driving experience much more so that we are currently.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The trials have also flagged potential problems. For example, many of the subjects who have tried the system out in Professor Dey&rsquo;s simulator have reported that it feels like playing a computer game &ndash; this &nbsp;detachment from reality is an issue that the engineers know needs to be addressed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;You don&rsquo;t want to lessen the impact of making a mistake,&rdquo; Professor Dey explains.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition, and perhaps most importantly, more work needs to be done to work out what is the optimum amount of the windscreen that can be covered with AR graphics, he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;After all, augmented reality is only useful if the driver is in touch with the reality behind it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","HeadlineLong":"Cars turn to augmented reality ","HeadlineShort":"Cars turn to augmented reality ","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Car firms hope to turn windscreens into giant computer displays that overlay the real world with useful information, such as directions or even social media feeds. \n","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Car firms hope turn windscreens into giant computer screens that overlay the real world with useful information, such as directions or even social media feeds. ","SummaryShort":"Windscreens become giant computer displays","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"6096a308-c201-4326-a503-b0419e1fe565","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121130-cars-turn-to-augmented-reality","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121130-cars-turn-to-augmented-reality"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121130-cars-turn-to-augmented-reality","_id":"559a6a1682f2d2dbf7bd47f3"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;As I said in my &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121115-the-cost-of-making-the-earth-move\"&gt;previous column&lt;/a&gt;, we are the biggest force in moving the planet&rsquo;s rocks and sediments around. Our global extractions are environmentally damaging and &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120418-no-silver-bullet\"&gt;depleting some resources&lt;/a&gt; to the extent that they are in danger of &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120308-touch-and-go-screens\"&gt;running out&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Many of those resources find their way into the goods, gadgets and machines that we find indispensable in our everyday lives. But do we really need so much stuff, or are we simply addicted to the new? There's no doubt that our consumption of resources from food to gadgets has risen dramatically over the past 60 years, and much of the world seems to be in the grip of a &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120712-beating-our-consumption-habit\"&gt;shopping epidemic&lt;/a&gt;. But is there a conscious effort driving it?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There have been many times during my travels when I've needed something repaired, from rips in my backpack, to holes in my clothes, zippers that have broken or memory cards that have lost data. From India to Ethiopia, I have had no trouble in finding someone who can sort the problem out, repair what is broken or find an ingenious way of side-stepping the issue. In rich countries, such items often would be thrown away and replaced with new ones without a second thought. But the developing world is still full of menders, make-doers and inspired users of others&rsquo; scrap. I&rsquo;ve seen a bicycle in Nairobi made from bits of car, a colander and a leather belt; aerials made from all manner of implements; and houses constructed out of old boat sails, rice sacks and plastic drinks bottles.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But then there are those items that seemingly can&rsquo;t be repaired. My camera shutter, battered by the dust and grime of travel, no longer works. I'm told I should throw away my camera, even though it works fine, apart from the shutter mechanism. Like the majority of consumer electronics, my camera has not been designed to be easily reparable. Thirty years ago, I could have found service manuals and spare parts for all camera models, as well as a thriving repair industry. But things have changed. Camera models have got far more numerous and complicated, and manufacturers no longer release repair manuals.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the mobile phone handset market reached saturation in Europe and the United States about a decade ago, we have chosen not to wait for our devices to fail. Almost all new phones purchased are &ldquo;upgrades&rdquo;, replacing functioning phones simply for reasons of fashion or for technological additions that many of us rarely use, and which could otherwise easily be achieved through software upgrades to existing handsets. Indeed, most phone companies compete for business by automatically upgrading customers&rsquo; phones every year. And these smart phones use mined resources from some of the most ecologically sensitive areas of the world, as a recent &lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=SE9KYQfctNk\"&gt;Friends of the Earth campaign&lt;/a&gt; points out.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Made to fail&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The idea that something that works fine should be replaced is now so ingrained in our culture that few people question it. But it is a fairly recent concept, brought about by a revolution in the advertising and manufacturing industries, which thrived on various 20th century changes, including the mass movement of large populations to cities, the development of mass production, globalisation, improved transport, international trade and public broadcast media.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The earliest example of manufacturers convincing people to frequently replace a product may be the so-called &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://documentaryheaven.com/the-lightbulb-conspiracy/\"&gt;lightbulb conspiracy&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo;, in which a group of companies is supposed to have orchestrated the Phoebus Cartel to prevent companies from selling lightbulbs with a longer than 1,000-hour lifespan (most settled on 750 hours), even though bulbs lasting more than 100,000 hours existed. The cartel said its intent was to develop international standards, but the net result was that households needed to replace their bulbs regularly, providing a far larger consumer market.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This way of selling more products by designing products that deliberately fail, cannot be repaired, or have a set lifespan imposed in some other way is known as planned obsolescence. However, it is not just a cynical plot dreamed up by manufacturers to boost profits, many politicians and economists believe it to be a societal necessity. The idea was born in the US during the 1930s depression as a way to get the economy moving again by compelling people to buy more stuff. There were plenty of factories and masses of unemployed looking for gainful work, the trouble was the people who could afford to buy things already had them. What was needed, strategists proposed, was a reason for shoppers to buy things they already had, or didn&rsquo;t know they &ldquo;needed&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;By the 1950s, planned obsolescence had become the dominant paradigm in mass production with things no longer built to last. A sophisticated advertising industry persuaded people to shop. Mechanisms flourished to make this easier, from department stores to credit. Consumerism was born. Some industries, such as fashion are predicated on planned obsolescence, with items being made to last a single season or less. Other industries are following fashion&rsquo;s high-turnover model and bringing out products that have cosmetic gimmicks or seasonal appeal but which will soon appear dated.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Computer says no&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Many people wonder whether electronic products are being designed to fail, from television sets, which have heat-sensitive condensers deliberately fitted onto the circuit board next to a heat sink connected to the transistors, to washing machines with ball-bearings fitted inaccessibly into the drums so they cannot be replaced. Specific lifespans are programmed by the manufacturers into chips in some equipment, so that printers will stop working after a preset number of pages (sending a message: &ldquo;internal error&rdquo;), coffee makers will cease functioning after a preset quota of brews, and memory cards will stop uploading after a preset number of photo uploads. The user is then forced to buy a replacement. Some computers are difficult to upgrade &ndash; new versions of software are upwardly compatible so files won&rsquo;t work on older models or, as in the new Apple MacBook Pro, the RAM is &lt;a href=\"http://ifixit.org/2763/the-new-macbook-pro-unfixable-unhackable-untenable/\"&gt;soldered to the motherboard&lt;/a&gt;, making it difficult or costly to upgrade or replace the hard drive and &lt;a href=\"http://www.pcmag.com/article2/0,2817,2405921,00.asp\"&gt;battery&lt;/a&gt;. As a result, users can find themselves buying a new computer every couple of years.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another trick is to make parts and accessories incompatible between brands or even models of the same brand. Thus, not only do consumers need to buy a different memory card or battery or charger for each device or brand of electronic equipment they buy, from phones to laptops to toothbrushes, but they must also &lt;a href=\"http://www.slate.com/articles/technology/technology/2012/09/iphone_5_dock_connector_the_one_incredibly_irksome_feature_that_will_leave_you_cursing_apple_.html\"&gt;buy a new charger or adaptor&lt;/a&gt; when they upgrade from an iPhone 4 to the iPhone 5, for example.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Some consumers are starting to hit back, though, advising people on the internet how to find and remove the printer chip or over-write the memory card software. But this is technically challenging and time-consuming, and most people aren&rsquo;t even aware of the reason for their device no longer working.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Californian Kyle Wiens is the type of sophisticated electronics tinkerer that emperors of the IT industry like Microsoft and Apple dream of employing. But Kyle is a guerilla geek &ndash; he slipped through their net and crossed over to the consumer side. Instead of dreaming up intricate ways of fitting more and more components into ever-slimmer gadgets, Kyle is taking them apart.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It started a decade ago, when he was a student and his iBook stopped working, Kyle explains. &ldquo;I couldn&rsquo;t get find a service manual for it, so as my friend [fellow student Luke Soules] and I took it apart to fix it, we created our own manual.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Kyle and Luke began taking apart computers and other equipment, getting around the copyright protection by creating their own service manuals from scratch, and posting them online for free. The pair called their site &lt;a href=\"http://ifixit.com/\"&gt;iFixit.com&lt;/a&gt; and over the past decade, they have expanded to hundreds more manuals, encouraging home hackers to create their own manuals for the site &ndash; &ldquo;we&rsquo;re a Wikipedia of service manuals&rdquo; &ndash; and they also sell the tools and spare parts users need to fix their own electrical devices.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;They are contributing to a cultural shift in perception, Kyle says. &ldquo;When someone has repaired their broken iPad or whatever, it&rsquo;s a life-changing experience. That person will become a different consumer. They will choose new products based on how long-lasting they are and how easy they are to take apart and repair.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Market forces&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At the moment, there is little onus on manufacturers to improve, and indeed things may be getting worse. As Apple&rsquo;s beautiful-looking and increasingly thin gadgets take an ever-larger share of the market, other manufacturers are following suit. &lt;a href=\"http://www.gsmarena.com/motorola_razr_maxx-review-800.php\"&gt;Motorola&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\"http://techcrunch.com/2012/04/15/nokia-lumia-900-review-this-ones-a-no-brainer/\"&gt;Nokia&lt;/a&gt;, both of which were known for their durable phones with easily replaced long-lasting batteries, have now both released thin phones with glued-in batteries.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But some companies have been bucking the trend. HP and Dell release service manuals and make computers that are easily upgradable and repairable &ndash; &lt;a href=\"http://ifixit.com/\"&gt;iFixit.com&lt;/a&gt; recently gave HPs Z1 model a score of 10 out of 10 for repairability.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And other companies are joining the move towards a circular economy, in which economic growth is uncoupled from finite-resource-use. Instead of the linear manufacturing route: mining materials, fabricating, selling, throwing them away; a circular economy is based around making products that are more easily disassembled, so that the resources can be recovered and used to make new products, &lt;a href=\"http://www.rreuse.org/t3/\"&gt;keeping them in circulation&lt;/a&gt;. British yachtswoman Ellen MacArthur is a strong advocate of the concept and &lt;a href=\"http://www.thecirculareconomy.org/\"&gt;commissioned a report into the idea&lt;/a&gt;, which found that the benefits to Europe&rsquo;s economy alone could be $630 billion, based on cycling just 15% of materials in 48% of manufacturing and just being recycled once.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The high-end outdoors clothing company &lt;a href=\"http://www.patagonia.com/email/11/112811.html\"&gt;Patagonia&lt;/a&gt;, for example, issues a guarantee that it will repair any of its products for free over their lifetime, and employs a team of seamstresses to do just that. And in 2011, it actually ran an advertising campaign asking consumers to buy less of the stuff they don&rsquo;t need, to curb waste and environmental damage.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As mines become depleted, it could soon force the market to change its recent ways. During the course of the 20th century there was a steady fall in the price of commodities, but that price has been climbing since 2000. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re moving towards a resource-limited economy, which means manufacturers are going to have to move their business model towards remanufacturing and repairs, as the cost of rare earth metals and other resources slash profits,&rdquo; Kyle says. Perhaps, then, planned obsolescence will begin to reach its own expiration date.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with Gaia?&lt;/em&gt; &lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a
