high cost of our throwaway culture","HeadlineShort":"The cost of our throwaway culture","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In the second of a two-part series, Gaia Vince explains how our consumer appetite is draining the planet of its resources, and why it is time to act more responsibly.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"In the second of a two-part series, Gaia Vince explains how consumerism is draining the planet of its resources, and why it is time to act more responsibly.","SummaryShort":"How we are draining the planet of its resources","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"64938ee8-34f9-4df5-8748-dcd5478a24ec","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121129-the-cost-of-our-throwaway-culture","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121129-the-cost-of-our-throwaway-culture"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121129-the-cost-of-our-throwaway-culture","_id":"559a6a1682f2d2dbf7bd47f1"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;If you&rsquo;re looking for a headline to capture our technological times, this recent one might fit the bill: &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://spectrum.ieee.org/geek-life/hands-on/the-diy-kidtracking-drone\"&gt;The DIY kid-tracking drone&lt;/a&gt;.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To help keep a close eye on his child, American parent Paul Wallich adapted a quadcopter to follow a GPS chip in his son&rsquo;s backpack to the bus stop every morning on the way to school: an ingenious outsourcing of parental responsibility, and a formidable piece of hacking to boot. What, though, does this say about the increasing delegation not only of daily tasks to machines, but of potentially life-changing decisions themselves?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Take a life and death dilemma that could soon be science fact rather than fiction. As psychology professor Gary Marcus pointed out in a recent &lt;a href=\"http://www.newyorker.com/online/blogs/newsdesk/2012/11/google-driverless-car-morality.html\"&gt;piece for &lt;em&gt;The New Yorker&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, driverless cars are now street-legal in three American states and could soon be cruising into a garage near you. But if your driverless car were hypothetically to encounter a bus full of schoolchildren on the wrong side of the road, how should it react: should it make a decision to swerve, preventing a collision but putting your life at risk; or should it collide with the bus if there&rsquo;s a greater chance of you surviving? &ldquo;If the decision must be made in milliseconds,&rdquo; Marcus reasons, &ldquo;the computer will have to make the call.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Cars are an interesting case study because they already represent one of the most hazardous technological environments most of us enter on a regular basis &ndash; and because the belief that technology should make this environment as safe as possible has been accepted, and been saving lives, for decades. As a famous adage of product design puts it, once you&rsquo;ve built the car, your task is not simply to hope that you can prevent all accidents, but to design a better car crash &ndash; that is, to make those inevitable occasions on which things go wrong as unlikely as possible to cause fatal harm.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Minimising harm is a clear enough good. What happens, though, to ethical issues in design when the product you&rsquo;re creating is itself going to be making decisions?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Killing machine&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Marcus&rsquo; driverless car scenario riffs off the famous &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trolley_problem\"&gt;trolley problem&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo;: a thought experiment that asks subjects to decide between pushing one man off a bridge in order to save the lives of several others trapped in the path of a runaway railway trolley, or allowing that one man to survive while the others meet their doom. Most respondents are unwilling physically to push another human being to their death even if it would save multiple lives &ndash; one of the factors the test is designed to measure. A machine&rsquo;s response, though, would depend entirely upon the decision-making model encoded by its creators. One robot might refuse to act; one might save the many; one might not even recognize a choice exists. It&rsquo;s up to us to decide which program to write. But once we&rsquo;ve done it and set things in motion, it&rsquo;s up to them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Programming this into cars is one thing. Weapons are quite another &ndash; and represent perhaps the most urgent testing-ground for machine ethics today. Early this December, Iran boasted that it had &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-middle-east-20594431\"&gt;captured a US ScanEagle drone&lt;/a&gt; over its airspace; one of the most basic models of the thousands of unmanned aerial vehicles that have become the standard stuff of warfare over the last decade.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Like Paul Wallich&rsquo;s child-minding quadcopter, most drones are more like sophisticated remote-controlled aircraft than autonomous decision-makers. Late November, though, saw some of the first tests of what &lt;a href=\"http://rt.com/usa/news/us-drone-launch-autonomous-986/\"&gt;some news outlets colourfully labelled a &ldquo;killer robot&rdquo;:&lt;/a&gt; a US Navy stealth drone piloted by artificial intelligence. Snappily named the X-47B Unmanned Combat Air System, the 19m- (62ft-) wide aircraft is designed to take split-second decisions on its own initiative &ndash; and even land itself on an aircraft carrier &ndash; while remaining under the overall control of human operators.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As much as anything, it&rsquo;s the relationship between these human operators and their subject that&rsquo;s most disturbing. Thought experiments like the trolley problem demonstrate something self-evident but extremely significant in moral thinking: how our sense of obligation is modified by distance and immediacy. We feel differently about pushing someone off a bridge with our own hands than we do about pushing a button to achieve the same result. Weapons are real-world examples of this: the arrow differs from the fist, the rifle from the arrow, the bomb from the gun. And by the time you reach autonomous systems, many of the &ldquo;actions&rdquo; setting them in motion will have taken place months or even years ago: as part of a process of programming and design whose eventual consequences may be entirely unknowable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If all this sounds rather removed from real life, it&rsquo;s worth remembering that the cutting edge of today&rsquo;s battlefield will represent little more than the state-of-the-art in domestic appliances a decade from now. Such is the speed of technological change, and the steady migration of its moral conundrums towards the mass market.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s a field in which the ghostly outlines of future controversy are already clearly visible. If a drone can oversee your child&rsquo;s journey to school today, will it be driving you both to work tomorrow &ndash; and should you insist on this, if the machine is demonstrably a safer driver than you? If robot soldiers can fight for your country without risking your sons&rsquo; and daughters&rsquo; lives, should you gratefully embrace automated warfare?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These are new questions, but answering them means grappling with ancient ethical issues about responsibility. What does it mean to hold someone responsible not only for their actions, but for the chains of consequence they set snaking through the world? And what does it mean to exercise human ingenuity responsibly, given the ever-expanding scope of our powers?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ironically, the best contemporary answer may mean giving up some of that power &ndash; and asking what it means to create machines that can take responsibility for themselves.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with Tom? If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","HeadlineLong":"Moral machines raise killer questions","HeadlineShort":"Moral machines, killer questions","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Who should take responsibility for decisions made by intelligent machines like killer drones or autonomous cars?\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Who should take responsibility for decisions made by intelligent machines like killer drones or autonomous cars?","SummaryShort":"Life and death dilemmas of intelligent robots","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"1276d154-16f7-4768-8075-9851a7d22074","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121206-moral-machines-killer-questions","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121206-moral-machines-killer-questions"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121206-moral-machines-killer-questions","_id":"559a6a1682f2d2dbf7bd4801"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;In the early morning of 16 June, 2012, a top secret spaceplane made a picture perfect landing at the Vandenberg Air Force Base in California. To those unfamiliar with the vehicle, it might have looked roughly similar to the US space shuttle, the manned spacecraft that shuttled astronauts into space for three decades.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But this spaceplane, called the X-37B Orbital Test Vehicle, is very different. While it looks like a plane, is launched on a rocket, has a cargo bay and uses some of the same technology as the shuttle, such as thermal shielding to protect it during reentry, it is smaller and unmanned. It is designed to stay in orbit for months on end and can automatically land back on Earth. Perhaps more crucially, the Boeing-designed plane is operated by the US Air Force and its mission is a closely held secret, prompting a slew of speculation about its true purpose.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the first X-37B was launched in 2010, amateur satellite spotters have carefully followed the robotic spacecraft&rsquo;s orbit, while those unconnected with the program have speculated that the plane could be anything from an anti-satellite weapon to so-called &ldquo;on demand reconnaissance,&rdquo; shorthand for a spy satellite that can be placed over any country in the world. Compounding the mystery was the launch of a second vehicle in 2011, which stayed in orbit for 469 days, long exceeding the Air Force&rsquo;s stated maximum requirement of 270 days for the spaceplane.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now, a third launch is slated for 11 December, according to an Air Force spokesperson, once again ramping up the rumour mill. So, what do we actually know about the plane?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tactical response&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Early reports focused on the X-37B&rsquo;s seeming resemblance, at least in size and weight, to &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing_X-20_Dyna-Soar\"&gt;the X-20 Dynasoar&lt;/a&gt; (short for Dynamic Soarer), a 1950s-era hypersonic vehicle that was envisioned for a variety of military missions, including bombing and sabotaging enemy satellites. However, experts familiar with the X-37B programme emphasized that its technology is actually closer to the recently retired space shuttle (a &lt;a href=\"http://pdf.aiaa.org/preview/2011/CDReadymspace11_2426/PV2011_7315.pdf\"&gt;fact reinforced by Boeings&rsquo; proposal for a crewed version of the vehicle&lt;/a&gt; known as the X-37C). The Air Force blandly described the role of the X-37B in &lt;a href=\"http://www.af.mil/information/factsheets/factsheet.asp?id=16639\"&gt;a factsheet given to media&lt;/a&gt; as a \"reliable, reusable, unmanned space test platform&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Air Force also says the mini-shuttle has two objectives: testing &ldquo;reusable spacecraft technologies&rdquo; and conducting &ldquo;experiments which can be returned to, and examined, on Earth&rdquo;. Again, this is similar to the stated aims of the space shuttle. But many forget that earlier craft also had a secret military role. Although ostensibly a civilian program, it conducted a series of missions from 1982-1992 on behalf of the National Reconnaissance Office, carrying a series of classified spy satellites.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Similarly, most outside experts now agree that it&rsquo;s likely the robotic space plane is being used for some sort of secret reconnaissance. &ldquo;I think the guess that makes most sense is quick-response tactical imaging, meaning hours to a couple of days from request to delivery,&rdquo; says Allen Thomson, a former CIA analyst.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thomson says it is also possible that it could have a more mundane but useful task, such as &ldquo;maintaining up-to-date general purpose mapping imagery.&rdquo; However, if that is the case, Thomson says that it could be a waste of money. &ldquo;I think that the commercial satellites could and should do that cheaper and better than X-37B,&rdquo; he says. It is a view &lt;a href=\"http://www.ucsusa.org/nuclear_weapons_and_global_security/space_weapons/technical_issues/the-x-37b-space-plane.html\"&gt;backed by parts of the scientific community&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Indeed, the X-37B launches comes in the middle of a larger debate about the role of government-operated spy satellites, which have proven enormously costly but can provide some of the most advanced imagery, versus commercial satellite imagery. The US intelligence community recently slashed its budget for commercial imagery, indicating that it was going back to greater reliance on its own classified satellites.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The view that the X-37B is a reconnaissance platform is strengthened by observations from amateur satellite watchers, who track the vehicles&rsquo; orbits, and noticed that it has similar orbits to spy satellites and scientific remote sensing craft. In addition, they noticed the craft changing its orbital path several times during its test flights.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is to be expected, says Joan Johnson-Freese, professor of national security studies at the US Naval War College in Newport. &ldquo;The upcoming launch will continue just to see what the vehicle can do,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;One of the things they are testing is maneuverability. The problem with satellites in orbit is they are very predictably in certain orbits at certain times, and thus vulnerable to anti-satellite weapons (ASAT).&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Johnson-Freese says the military has long been interested in the ability of a spacecraft that has &ldquo;the ability to evade, to maneuver, to not be in a predicable place at a predicable time.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;She expects the Air Force will be &ldquo;pressing the envelope for manoeuvrability and duration&rdquo; during the X-37B&rsquo;s next flight. &ldquo;That will give them the idea of potential missions where avoidance of ASATs comes into play.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;No weapons&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although its orbits may be difficult to predict in advance, its tracks show where the X-37B has been and its likely purpose, says Brian Weeden, a technical advisor at the Secure World Foundation, a Washington-based foundation that focuses on space issues.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For example, he says, the X-37B flew at inclination of 42.79 degrees, which tells you how far north and south in latitude the spacecraft can see. &ldquo;The tradeoff is that something at a 90 degree polar orbit covers the whole world, but its frequency is less; it may arrive only every couple of days,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;If something is 40 or 45 degrees, it would be covering a smaller portion [of the earth], but more often. &ldquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At a 42.79 degree inclination, the X-37B would be useful for looking at a geographic region such as the Middle East, says Weeden, pouring cold water on one theory that that it was used to spy on &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/science-environment-16423881\"&gt;China&rsquo;s spacelab, Tiangong-1&lt;/a&gt;. And given the current political context, he says, the Middle East &ldquo;makes sense&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Weeden also suggests the X-37B&rsquo;s orbit may indicate that the military is trying out a new sensor system, such as radar imaging or &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperspectral_imaging\"&gt;hyperspectral sensors&lt;/a&gt;, which collect information across different wavelengths. He suggests this could be the case, because unlike satellites collecting light in the visible wavelength, the X-37B&rsquo;s orbits are not synchronized with the sun, a trick used to maintain a predictable angle between the sun, satellite and ground.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But, like with many of the theories surrounding the X-37B, he warns, &ldquo;it is just speculation.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For its part, the Air Force itself is silent about the plane&rsquo;s use, only speaking to allay fears that it was a weapon. \"I don't know how this could be called weaponisation of space. It's just an updated version of the space shuttle type of activities in space,\" said Gary Payton, the Air Force's deputy undersecretary for space programmes, in 2010.&nbsp; \"We, the Air Force, have a suite of military missions in space and this new vehicle could potentially help us do those missions better.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Whatever its purpose; the X-37B is perhaps one of the few bright spots among the Pentagon&rsquo;s hypersonic test vehicle programs.&nbsp; The Air Force&rsquo;s X-51A Waverider, a scramjet powered hypersonic missile, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120815-hypersonic-ambitions-fall-short\"&gt;suffered a fatal mishap earlier this year on a test flight&lt;/a&gt;, and never reached hypersonic speeds.&nbsp; Separately, another hypersonic prototype, known as the Falcon Hypersonic Test Vehicle-2, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-us-canada-14497641\"&gt;suffered mishaps in both flight tests&lt;/a&gt;, plunging into the ocean.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Those other efforts, which are focused on creating missiles, are still in early testing phase, while the X-37B is clearly further along.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I think the one interesting question is whether this is just test and evaluation, or is it being used to be support real world operations,&rdquo; says Weeden. But like most questions about the plane, it is one that is currently impossible to answer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","HeadlineLong":"X-37B: Secrets of the US military spaceplane","HeadlineShort":"Secrets of US military spaceplane","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"As the US Air Force prepares for the third launch of its mysterious X-37B vehicle, BBC Future looks at what is known about the classified project.                  <p class="introduction">
                  The Consumer Electronics Show (CES)  one of the worlds largest fairs dedicated to technology  opened in Las Vegas this week.
                 </p>

           <div class="clearfix" id="gallery-list-wrapper">
            <div class="page-component-wrapper">
             <div class="bottom-unit">
              <div class="body-content">
               <p>
                The annual gathering has become a Mecca for gadget fans, attracting more than 150,000 people to wander its 170,000 sq m (1.85m sq ft) of floorspace in search of the latest trends amongst the 20,000 new products debuted each year.
               </p>
               <p>
                In fact, it has grown so big that
                <a href="http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20130104-does-ces-have-a-future">
                 some now question whether it is still relevant
                </a>
                and whether it has a future.
               </p>
               <p>
                But over the last four decades, the annual trade show has undeniably introduced the world to many
                <a href="http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20130107-ces-hits-and-misses">
                 of the consumer electronic trends that shaped our world.. and many that didnt
                </a>
                .
               </p>
               <p>
                How did it grow so big and why does it attract so much attention every year?
               </p>
               <p>
                Explore these retro pictures of the CES of yesteryear.
               </p>
               <p>
                <strong>
                 More stunning image galleries:
                </strong>
               </p>
               <p>
                <a href="http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20130104-most-stunning-images-of-the-week">
                 Gruesome tools and intimate views
                </a>
                <strong>
                 <br/>
                </strong>
               </p>
               <p>
                <a href="http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121221-most-stunning-images-of-the-week">
                 Christmas treats and the end of the world
                </a>
               </p>
               <p>
                <a href="http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121213-most-stunning-images-of-the-week">
                 Bathing snow monkeys, a tweeting pope and elephant dung coffee.
                </a>
               </p>
               <p>
                <a href="http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121207-most-stunning-images-of-the-week">
                 Architectural giants and a new way of seeing the Moon
                </a>
               </p>
               <p>
                <a href="http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121129-most-stunning-images-of-the-week">
                 Red pandas, Saturn and Natures unstoppable forces
                </a>
               </p>
               <p>
                <a href="http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121123-pictures-of-the-week">
                 The world's largest bloom, the world's largest aquarium and the world's fastest sailing boat
                </a>
               </p>
               <p>
                <a href="http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121116-best-images-of-the-week">
                 Venice under water, a total eclipse and a pedestrian-avoiding car
                </a>
               </p>
               <p>
                <a href="http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121030-octobers-best-images">

                </a>
               </p>

  <script type="text/javascript">
   document.write('&lt;' + 'script id="orb-js-script" data-assetpath="http://static.bbci.co.uk/frameworks/barlesque/2.83.10/orb/4/" src="http://static.bbci.co.uk/frameworks/barlesque/2.83.10/orb/4/script' + (( document.cookie.indexOf('ckns_debugorbjs') &gt; -1 )? '-debug' : '') + '/orb.js"&gt;&lt;' + '/script&gt;');
  </script>
  <script type="text/javascript">
   (function() {
    'use strict';

    var promoManager = {
        url: '',
        segments: ['a', 'b'],
        promoLoaded: false,
                makeUrl: function (variant, theme, win) {
            var loc = win? win.location : window.location,
                proto = loc.protocol,
                host = loc.host,
                url = proto + '//' + ((proto.match(/s:/i) &amp;&amp; !host.match(/^www\.(int|test)\./i))? 'ssl.' : 'www.'),
                themes = ['light', 'dark'];

            if ( host.match(/^(?:www|ssl)\.(int|test|stage|live)\.bbc\./i) ) {
                url += RegExp.$1 + '.';
            }
            else if ( host.match(/^pal\.sandbox\./i) ) {
                url += 'test.';
            }
 
                        theme = themes[ +(theme === themes[0]) ];
           
           return url + 'bbc.co.uk/navpromo/' + variant + '/' + theme;
        },
                validSegment: function (segment) {
            var validSegments = this.segments;
            
            for (var i = 0, len = validSegments.length; i &lt; len; i++) {
                if (validSegments[i] === segment) {
                    return segment;
                }
            }
            
            return validSegments[0];
        },
                init: function(node) {
            var disabledByCookie = (document.cookie.indexOf('ckns_orb_nopromo=1') &gt; -1),
                orbFullWidth     = (document.getElementById('orb-aside').offsetWidth &gt;= 1008),
                that = this;
            
            if (window.promomanagerOverride) {
                for (var p in promomanagerOverride) {
                    that[p] = promomanagerOverride[p];
                }
            }
                
            if ( window.orb.fig('uk') &amp;&amp; orbFullWidth &amp;&amp; !disabledByCookie ) {
                require(['orb/async/_footerpromo', 'istats-1'], function(promo, istats) {
                                        var mandolinEndDate = new Date().getTime() + (7 * 60 * 60 * 24) * 1000,
                        mandolin = new bbc.Mandolin('footer-promo', that.segments, {rate: 0.2, end: mandolinEndDate}),
                        segmentToRequest = that.validSegment(mandolin.getSegment());

                    that.url = (window.promomanagerOverride || that).makeUrl(segmentToRequest, 'light');

                    if (that.url) { 
                        promo.load(that.url, node, {
                                                          onSuccess: function(e) {
                                istats.addLabels({ 'campaignID': e.campaignID });
                                if (segmentToRequest === mandolin.getSegment()) {
                                    istats.addLabels({ 'promo_id_segment': e.campaignID + ':' + mandolin.getSegment() });
                                }
                                istats.track('internal', {region: node, linkLocation : 'orb-footer-promo'});

                                istats.log('display', 'orb-footer-promo-displayed', {campaignID : e.campaignID, testVariant: segmentToRequest});
                                node.className = node.className + ' orb-footer-promo-loaded';
                                promoManager.promoLoaded = true;
                                promoManager.event('promo-loaded').fire(e);
                             },
                             onError: function() {
                                istats.log('error', 'orb-footer-promo-failed');
                                document.cookie = 'ckns_orb_nopromo=1; expires=' + new Date(new Date().getTime() + 1000 * 60 * 10).toGMTString() + ';';
                             }
                        });   
                    }
                });
            }
        }
    };
    
        
    define('orb/promomanager', ['orb/lib/_event'], function (event) {
        event.mixin(promoManager);
        return promoManager;
    });
    
    require(['orb/promomanager'], function (promoManager) {
        promoManager.init(document.getElementById('orb-footer-promo'));
    })
})();
  </script>

  </script>
